Produced by John Bickers, and Dagny

BEATRIX

By Honore De Balzac

Translated by Katharine Prescott Wormeley

NOTE

  Remarkable as it may seem, Balzac, who dealt with character and minute circumstances of life, was seldom accused of representing actual persons in the two or three thousand portraits he painted of human nature.

  In “The Great Man of the Provinces in Paris” some likenesses were imagined: Jules Janin in Etienne Lousteau, Armand Carrel in Michel Chrestien, and possibly Berryer in Daniel d’Arthez. But in this volume, “Beatrix,” he used characteristics of certain persons, recognized and admitted at the time of publication. Mademoiselle des Touches (Camille Maupin) is George Sand in character, and the personal description of her, though applied by some to the famous Mademoiselle Georges, is easily recognized from Couture’s drawing. Beatrix, Conti, and Claude Vignon sketch the Comtesse d’Agoult, Liszt, and the critic Gustave Planche.

  The opening scene, representing the manners and customs of the old Breton family and the transition of the vieille roche into present customs and ideas, is one of Balzac’s remarkable and famous pictures in the “Comedy of Human Life.”

  K.P.W.

BEATRIX

I. A BRETON TOWN AND MANSION

France, especially Brittany, still contains towns outside the movement that gives the nineteenth century its peculiar characteristics. For lack of quick, regular communication with Paris and poor roads to the arrondissement or provincial capital, these towns regard new civilization as a spectacle to be gazed at; it amazes them, but they do not embrace it. Whether they fear or scoff, they remain faithful to old manners and customs. A moral archaeologist observing men rather than stones would find images of Louis XV’s time in many a village of Provence, of Louis XIV’s in the depths of Poitou, and still older types in Brittany. Most of these towns have fallen from splendors not mentioned by historians, who care more for facts than for the truer history of manners. Tradition preserves that splendor in Brittany, where the native character forgets nothing that concerns its land. Many towns were once capitals of little feudal states—counties or duchies conquered by the crown or divided when the male line failed. Disinherited from active life, these heads became arms; arms without nourishment wither and scarcely vegetate.

For the last thirty years these pictures of ancient times have begun to fade. Modern industry, working for the masses, destroys creations of ancient art, whose works were once as personal to the consumer as to the artisan. Nowadays we have products, not works. Public buildings, monuments of the past, count in retrospection; but monuments of modern industry are freestone quarries, saltpetre mines, cotton factories. A few years more and even these old cities will be transformed and seen only in this iconography.

One of the towns preserving the feudal type most accurately is Guerande. The name awakens memories in painters, artists, and thinkers who have visited the slopes on which this splendid jewel of feudality lies posed to command the tides and dunes—the summit, as it were, of a triangle whose other corners are Croisic and the village of Batz. Few towns, except Vitre and Avignon, preserve so intact the medieval type into our epoch.

Guerande is still encircled with doughty walls; its moats are full, battlements entire, loopholes unencumbered with vegetation; even ivy has never mantled the towers. The town has three gates where rings of portcullises are seen; it is entered by an iron-clamped drawbridge, no longer raised but which could be. The mayoralty was blamed in 1820 for planting poplars along the moat to shade the promenade, excusing it by noting the fortifications’ esplanade had been converted a century earlier into a mall where inhabitants took pleasure beneath the elms.

The houses have suffered no change; none have altered their frontage, had stories added, or been plastered by an architect. Some rest on wooden columns forming arcades beneath which foot-passengers circulate, floor planks bending but never breaking. Merchant houses are small and low, their fronts veneered with slate. Decaying wood figures in carved window-casings and pillars, with grotesque faces looking down and fantastic beasts climbing the angles—relics that resist change and present dusky tones and half-blurred features painters delight in.

The streets are what they were four hundred years ago—with one exception: population no longer swarms. Social movement is so dead that a traveler may walk alone, not without sadness, through a deserted street where mullioned windows are plastered up to avoid the window-tax. This street ends at a postern flanked with a masonry wall, beyond which rises a bouquet of trees planted by Breton nature, one of the most luxuriant vegetations in France. A painter or poet might sit there silently to taste the quiet beneath the arch of the postern, where no voice comes from the city’s life and the landscape is seen through the loop-holes of the casemates once occupied by halberdiers and archers, not unlike the sashes of some belvedere arranged for a point of view.

One cannot walk through the place without thinking at every step of habits and usages of long-past times; the very stones tell of them. The ideas of the middle ages remain with their superstitions. If by chance a gendarme passes with his silver-laced hat, his presence is an anachronism; but modern objects are rare. There is little of contemporary clothing; what exists is adapted to immutable customs and unchanging physiognomies. The public square is filled with Breton costumes, which artists flock to draw; these stand out in relief on the scene. The whiteness of the linen worn by the paludiers (salt-gatherers) contrasts with the blues and browns of peasants and the sacredly preserved jewelry of the women. These classes and the sailors with jerkins and varnished leather caps are as distinct as castes and still recognize their distance from bourgeoisie, nobility, and clergy. Lines are clearly marked; the revolutionary level found the masses too rugged and hard to plane. The immutability attributed by science to zoological species is found in Breton human nature. Even after the Revolution of 1830, Guerande remained a town apart, essentially Breton, fervently Catholic, silent and self-contained—a place where modern ideas have little access.

Its geographical position explains this. The pretty town overlooks a salt-marsh producing Guerande salt, to which many Bretons attribute the excellence of their butter and sardines. It is connected to the rest of France by two roads only: one from Savenay, which stops at Saint-Nazaire; a second from Vannes connecting it with the Morbihan. The arrondissement road establishes land communication, and from Saint-Nazaire by water with Nantes. The land road is used only by government; the more frequent way is by water from Saint-Nazaire. Between this village and Guerande is eighteen miles the mail-coach does not serve; not three coach passengers a year would pass over it.

These obstacles discourage travelers. Government is slow; inhabitants readily endure difficulties that separate them from the rest of France. Guerande, at the continent’s extreme end, leads nowhere, and no one comes there. Glad to be ignored, she thinks and cares about herself. The immense product of her salt-marshes, which pays a tax of not less than a million to the Treasury, is chiefly managed at Croisic, a peninsular village communicating with Guerande over quicksands that erase tracks at night, and by boats crossing the arm of the sea that makes Croisic’s port.

This town is the Herculaneum of feudality, less its lava. It is afoot but not living; it survives because it has not been demolished. If you reach Guerande from Croisic after crossing dreary salt-marshes, you will experience a strong sensation at sight of that vast fortification. From Saint-Nazaire its picturesque position and naive grace please no less. The country about it is ravishing: hedges full of flowers, honeysuckles, roses, box, and enchanting plants. It is like an English garden designed by a great architect. This rich, coy nature, untrodden with the grace of violets or lilies in a forest glade, is framed by an African desert banked by the ocean—a desert without tree, herb, or bird; where on sunny days laboring paludiers, clothed in white and scattered among melancholy swamps, remind one of Arabs in burrows.

Thus Guerande bears no resemblance to any other place in France. The town produces upon the mind the effect of a sleeping‑draught. It is silent as Venice. There is no public conveyance beyond the springless wagon of a carrier who carries travellers, merchandise, and occasionally letters from Saint-Nazaire to Guerande and back. Bernus, the carrier, was, in 1829, the factotum of this large community. He went and came when he pleased; all the country knew him; he did errands for all. The arrival of a carriage in Guerande, that of a lady or some invalid going to Croisic for sea-bathing (thought more efficacious among those rocks than at Boulogne or Dieppe) is still an immense event. Peasants come on horseback, most with commodities for barter in sacks, induced by the need to purchase jewels distinctive of their caste which are given to Breton brides, and the white linen or cloth for their clothing.

For a circuit ten miles round, Guerande is always GUERANDE—the town where the famous treaty was signed in 1365, the key of the coast, which may boast, not less than Batz, of a splendor lost in time. The jewels, linen, cloth, ribbon, and hats are made elsewhere, but to those who buy them they are from Guerande. Artists, and even certain bourgeois, who come to Guerande feel, as at Venice, a desire (soon forgotten) to end their days amid its peace and silence, walking along the beautiful mall that surrounds the town from gate to gate toward the sea. Sometimes the image of this town rises in the temple of memory; she enters crowned with towers, clasped by her girdle; her flower-strewn robe floats, the golden mantle of her dunes enfolds her, and the fragrant breath of her briony paths exhales at every step; she fills your mind like some enchanting woman whose presence still lingers in a fold of your heart.

Near the church of Guerande stands a mansion to the town what the town is to the region—an exact image of the past, the symbol of a great thing destroyed—a poem. This mansion belongs to the noblest family of the province, the du Guaisnics, who in the days of du Guesclins were as superior in antiquity and fortune as the Trojans to the Romans. The Guaisnics (also spelled du Glaicquin), from which du Guesclin comes, are old as Brittany’s granite. They are neither Frenchmen nor Gauls—they are Bretons, Celts. Formerly they must have been Druids, gathering mistletoe in sacred forests and sacrificing men upon dolmens. Whatever their origin, today this pure race, equal to the Rohans without having made themselves princes and powerful before the ancestors of Hugues Capet, possesses two thousand francs a year, its mansion in Guerande, and the little castle of Guaisnic. All lands belonging to the barony of Guaisnic, the first in Brittany, are pledged to farmers and bring in sixty thousand francs a year despite ignorant culture. The du Guaisnics remain nominal owners although they receive none of the revenues, because for two hundred years they have been unable to pay off advances. They are in the position of the crown towards its engagistes before 1789. Where and when could the barons obtain the million their farmers advanced? Before 1789 the fiefs under Guaisnic were worth fifty thousand francs a year; but a vote of the National Assembly suppressed seigneurs’ dues on inheritance.

In such a situation this family—of no account in France and a subject of laughter in Paris—was in Guerande the whole of Brittany. In Guerande the Baron du Guaisnic is one of the great barons of France, a man above whom there is but one— the King. Today the name du Guaisnic, full of Breton significances (roots explained in “The Chouans”), has been altered as other names have been; the tax-gatherer now writes du Guenic.

At the end of a silent, damp, gloomy lane stands an arch of a gate wide enough for a man on horseback—proof that when built carriages did not exist. The arch, supported by two jambs, is of granite. The gate, of oak, rugged as bark, is studded with enormous nails in geometric figures. The semicircular arch is carved with the arms of the Guaisnics as clean-cut as though the sculptor had just laid down his chisel. This escutcheon delights lovers of heraldry by a simplicity proving the family’s pride and antiquity. It is as in crusaders’ days when Christian world invented symbols to recognize one another; the Guaisnics have never quartered it. Here, as at Guerande, it reads: Gules, a hand proper gonfaloned ermine, with a sword argent in pale, and the motto, FAC. Is not that a grand and noble thing? A baronial coronet surmounts this simple escutcheon. The artist has given some proud, chivalrous turn to the hand. With what vigor it holds the sword that recently served the family!

If you go to Guerande after reading this history you cannot fail to quiver at that blazon. Even the most confirmed republican would be moved by the fidelity, nobleness, and grandeur hidden in that dark lane. The du Guaisnics did well yesterday, and are ready to do well tomorrow. To DO is chivalry’s motto. “You did well in the battle” was the praise of the Connetable par excellence, the great du Guesclin who drove the English for a time from France. The depth of carving, protected from weather by the arch, matches the moral depth of the motto in the family’s soul. To those who know the Guaisnics this fact is touching.

The gate, when open, gives a vista into a somewhat vast courtyard, on the right the stables, on the left the kitchen and offices. The house is built of freestone from cellar to garret. The facade on the court-yard has a portico with a double range of steps, its wall covered with carved vestiges now effaced by time, but an antiquary can still make out the Hand bearing the sword in the center. Granite steps are disjointed, grasses and moss force their way through fissures, yet solidity remains. The door must have had a charming character. From remnants of the designs one can judge it was by an artist of the great Venetian school of the thirteenth century. A mixture appears of Byzantine and Saracenic. It is crowned with a circular pediment now wreathed in vegetation—a bouquet rose, brown, yellow, or blue by season. The oak, nail-studded door opens to a noble hall, at the end of which is another door opening to a portico that leads to the garden.

This hall is marvelously well preserved. Panelled wainscot three feet high is of chestnut. A magnificent Spanish leather with figures in relief, gilding now peeled or reddened, covers the walls. The ceiling is of wooden boards artistically joined and painted; the gold is faint; a few red flowers and green foliage remain. Perhaps thorough cleaning might reveal paintings like those discovered on Tristan’s house at Tours, proving the planks date or were restored in Louis XI’s reign. The chimney-piece is enormous carved stone; within are giant andirons of wrought-iron, precious workmanship. It could hold a cart-load of wood. Furniture is wholly oak, each article bearing the family arms. Three English guns fit for chase or war, three sabres, two game-bags and fishermen’s utensils hang from nails on the wall.

On one side is a dining-room connecting with the kitchen by a door cut through a corner tower. This tower corresponds on the facade toward the court-yard with another tower at the opposite corner, where a spiral staircase leads to the two upper stories.

The dining-room hangs with tapestries of the fourteenth century; the style and orthography of inscriptions on the banderols beneath each figure prove their age, but in the naive language of the fabliaux they are impossible to transcribe here. These tapestries, preserved where light has scarcely penetrated, are framed in bands of oak black as ebony. The ceiling has projecting rafters enriched with varied foliage; spaces between are planks painted blue where garlands of golden flowers twine. Two old buffers face each other; on shelves rubbed by Mariotte the cook, can be seen as in 1200, four old goblets, an ancient embossed soup-tureen, two silver salt-cellars; many pewter plates and pitchers of gray and blue pottery with arabesque designs and the du Guaisnics’ arms, covered by hinged pewter lids. The chimney-piece is modernized—its state proves the family has lived in this room for the last century. It is of carved stone in Louis XV style, ornamented with a mirror let in the back with gilt beaded moulding. This anachronism, to which the family is indifferent, would grieve a poet. On the mantel-shelf, covered with red velvet, stands a tall tortoise-shell clock inlaid with brass, flanked by silver candelabra of singular design. A large square table with solid legs fills the center; the chairs are turned wood covered with tapestry. On a round table supported by a single leg in the shape of a vine-shoot before a window into the garden is an odd lamp: a common glass globe about the size of an ostrich egg fastened to a candlestick by a glass tube; a wick issues through a hole at the top and passes through a brass reed drawing nut-oil coiled within like a tape-worm in a surgeon’s phial. Windows into the garden and court-yard are mullioned with hexagonal panes, draped by curtains with heavy valances of ancient crimson silk with gold reflections, once called brocatelle or small brocade.

Each of the two upper stories has but two rooms: the head of the family’s bedroom and the children’s. Guests lodged in chambers beneath the roof; servants above kitchens and stables. The pointed roof, protected with lead at angles and edges, has noble pointed windows on each side, one looking down on the court-yard, the other on the garden. These windows rise almost to the roof and have slender casings whose carvings have crumbled under salty vapors. Above each arch with stone crossbars grinds the vane of a noble.

Let us note a precious detail, full of naivete, valuable to an archaeologist. The spiral-stair tower is placed at the corner of a great gable wall with no window. The staircase descends to a little arched door opening on a gravelled yard between house and stables. This tower is repeated on the garden side by another of five sides ending in a cupola containing a bell-turret, instead of being roofed like the sister-tower with a pepper-pot. Architects varied symmetry of skylines thus. These towers connect on the first floor by a stone gallery supported by brackets each ending in a grotesque human head. The gallery has an exquisite balustrade. From the gable hangs a stone dais like those crowning statues of saints at church portals. Can you not see a woman walking on this balcony and gazing over Guerande at sunshine gilding the sands and shimmering on Ocean? Admire the gable wall flanked at its angles with those varied towers. The opposite gable of the Guaisnic mansion adjoins the next house. Harmony so carefully sought by architects of those days is maintained on the court-yard facade by the tower communicating between dining-room and kitchen, the same as the staircase tower except it stops at the first upper story and its summit is a small open dome beneath which stands a now blackened statue of Saint Calyste.

The garden is magnificent for so old a place. It covers half an acre, its walls espaliered, the space divided into vegetable squares bordered with cordons of fruit-trees that the man-of-all-work, Gasselin, tends between grooming horses. At the garden’s farther end is a grotto with a seat; in the middle, a sun-dial; the paths are gravelled. The garden facade has no towers corresponding to those on the court-yard; but a slender spiral column rises from ground to roof that once bore the family banner—an iron socket at its summit where a few weak plants straggle. This detail, with vestiges of sculpture, proves to a practiced eye that a Venetian architect built the mansion. The graceful staff is a signature revealing Venice, chivalry, and thirteenth-century delicacy. If any doubt remained, the ornamentation’s trefoils with four leaves instead of three would dispel it. This difference plainly indicates the Venetian school depraved by commerce with the East, where semi-Saracenic architects, careless of Catholic thought, give four leaves to clover, while Christian art is faithful to the Trinity. Here Venetian art becomes heretical.

If this ancient dwelling attracts your imagination, you may ask why such miracles are not renewed. Because today mansions are sold, pulled down, and their grounds turned into streets. No one is sure the next generation will possess the paternal dwelling; homes are no more than inns; whereas in former times when a dwelling was built, men worked, or thought they worked, for a family in perpetuity. Hence the grandeur of these houses. Faith in self, as well as faith in God, performed prodigies.

As for the arrangement of the upper rooms, they may be imagined after this ground-floor description and an account of the family’s manners, customs, and physiognomy. For the last fifty years the du Guaisnics have received friends in the two rooms described, in which, as in the court-yard and external accessories, the spirit, grace, and candor of old noble Brittany still survive. Without the town’s topography and the house’s minute depiction, the surprising figures of the family might be less understood. Therefore the frames have preceded the portraits. Everyone knows things influence beings. There are public buildings whose effect is visible upon persons living nearby. It would be difficult to be irreligious in the shadow of a cathedral like Bourges. When the soul is everywhere reminded of its destiny by surrounding images, it is less easy to fail of it. Such was our immediate grandfathers’ thought, now abandoned by a generation soon to have no signs, no distinctions, and whose manners and morals would change every decade. If you do not now expect to find the Baron du Guaisnic sword in hand, all here written would be falsehood.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Early in May 1836, when this scene opens, the family of Guenic (we adopt the modern spelling) consisted of Monsieur and Madame du Guenic, Mademoiselle du Guenic, the baron’s elder sister, and an only son, aged twenty-one, named Gaudebert-Calyste-Louis by an old family usage; the father’s name was Gaudebert-Calyste-Charles. Only the last name ever varied. Saint Gaudebert and Saint Calyste were forever bound to protect the Guenics.

The Baron du Guenic had left Guerande the moment La Vendée and Brittany rose in arms; he fought through the war with Charette, Cathelineau, La Rochejaquelein, d’Elbée, Bonchamps, and the Prince de Loudon. Prudently, before leaving, he had sold all his property to his elder sister, Mademoiselle Zéphirine du Guenic. After the death of those leaders, the baron, miraculously spared, refused to submit to Napoleon and continued to resist until 1802, when, defeated and nearly captured, he returned to Guerande, went to Croisic, and from there crossed to Ireland, faithful to the old Breton hatred for England.

The people of Guerande feigned ignorance of the baron’s existence; in twenty years not a single indiscreet word was uttered. Mademoiselle du Guenic collected the rents and sent them to her brother by fishermen. Monsieur du Guenic returned to Guerande in 1813 as quietly as if he had spent a season at Nantes. During his stay in Dublin the old Breton, well past fifty, fell in love with a charming Irishwoman, Fanny O’Brien, daughter of a noble but poor family. She was twenty-one. The baron went to France to obtain marriage papers, returned to Ireland, and about ten months later (early 1814) brought his wife to Guerande, where she gave him Calyste on the very day Louis XVIII landed at Calais—hence the young man’s final name, Louis.

The old and loyal Breton was now seventy-three; his long guerrilla warfare, exile, and perilous sea crossings had aged him prematurely, making him seem a hundred. Thus the chief of the house of Guenic fitted perfectly with the worn-out grandeur of their dwelling, built when a court still reigned at Guerande.

Monsieur du Guenic was tall, straight, wiry, and lean. His oval face was lined with innumerable wrinkles that formed a net over cheekbones and brow, resembling the faces Rembrandt and others loved to paint. His countenance seemed sunken beneath those wrinkles, produced by outdoor life and watching his country. Yet enough remained of the human face to touch the soul. The firm outline, the shape of the brow, the solemn lines, the rigid nose, the bony structure—wounded only slightly—declared intrepidity without calculation, faith without reserve, obedience without discussion, fidelity without compromise, and love without inconstancy. In him, Breton granite had become man.

The baron had no teeth; his lips, once red, were now violet and drawn inward, giving an expression both threatening and proud. His chin seemed to seek his nose, which, humped in the middle, showed his energy and Breton resistance. His skin, marbled with red blotches through the wrinkles, indicated a strongly sanguine temperament fitted to resist fatigue. His hair was abundant and white, falling in curls on his shoulders. In his black eyes, despite the extinguished face, still glittered the last flames of a generous and loyal soul. Eyebrows and lashes had disappeared; shaving had become difficult, so he let his beard grow fan-shaped. An artist would have admired his splendid hands—large, broad, hairy, the hands of a soldier and guerilla, hands that had handled sword and oar, that had fought among thorns and marshes. Looking closely one might see recent marks attesting that the Baron had again joined the Vendée. These hands were a living commentary on the Guenic motto: Fac!

His forehead showed golden temples against a hard narrow brow, broadened by hair loss, adding majesty to that noble ruin. The countenance, a little material perhaps but natural to the Breton type, presented a certain savagery and stolid calm, resembling Huguenot impassibility—a kind of repose where animal nature dominates and thought is rare. Thought seemed to be an effort; its seat was more in the heart than the head; it led to acts rather than ideas. Examined closely, the mystery resolves: he had faiths and sentiments inborn, allowing him to dispense with much thought. Institutions and religion thought for him; he reserved his mind, and his kind, for action. He drew thought from his heart like his sword from its scabbard, held aloft on his scutcheon, shining with sincerity. That explains the sale to his sister before the war—provision for all eventualities: death, confiscation, exile. The beauty of character in these two old people (for the sister lived only by and for the brother) cannot be understood by the selfish morals of the present age. An archangel could not have found one thought of personal interest. In 1814, when the rector of Guerande suggested the baron go to Paris to claim recompense from the Bourbons, the sister cried, “Oh, fy! does my brother need to hold out his hand like a beggar?” “It would be thought I served a king from interest,” said the baron. “Besides, it is for him to remember. Poor king! he must be weary indeed of those who harass him.”

This loyal servant, who had spent life and means on Louis XVIII, received the rank of colonel, the cross of Saint-Louis, and a stipend of two thousand francs a year—news the baron accepted as the king’s remembrance. No one undeceived him; the gift had been made by the Duc de Feltre. As gratitude to the king, the baron held a siege at Guerande against General Travot; refusing to surrender the fortress, he evacuated into the woods with a band of Chouans who remained armed until the second Bourbon restoration. Guerande still remembers that siege.

The Baron du Guenic was as illiterate as a peasant; he could read, write, and cipher a little, knew military art and heraldry, but, aside from his prayer-book, had not read three volumes in his life. His clothing was always the same: stout shoes, ribbed stockings, greenish velveteen breeches, a cloth waistcoat, and a loose coat with a collar from which hung the cross of Saint-Louis. A noble serenity reigned on his face; for the last year or so sleep—prelude to death—seemed preparing him for eternal rest. This somnolence did not alarm his wife, his blind sister, or his friends, whose medical knowledge was slight; to them these pauses were natural: the baron had done his duty.

In this ancient mansion the absorbing interests were the fortunes of the dispossessed elder branch, the future of the exiled Bourbons, the Catholic religion, and political innovations in Brittany. The one personal interest was the attachment of all for the only son, Calyste, heir and sole hope of the du Guenic name.

Some years earlier the old Vendean had a return of youth to train his son in manly exercises proper for a gentleman ready at any moment to take arms. When Calyste was sixteen, his father took him to the marshes and forest, teaching war’s rudiments through chase, preaching by example, firm in saddle and sure in shot, indifferent to fatigue, intrepid before obstacles, bidding his son follow him into danger.

So, when the Duchesse de Berry landed to attempt restoration for her son, the father judged it right to take his boy and join her, practicing the family motto. The baron started at night, saying nothing to his wife, taking his son under fire as if to a fête, accompanied by Gasselin, his sole vassal. The three were absent three months without sending news to the baroness, who read the Quotidienne with trembling, nor to the blind sister, whose nerve never faltered. The three guns on the walls had seen service recently. The baron, considering the enterprise useless, left the region before the affair of La Pénissière; otherwise the house of Guenic might have ended in hecatomb.

On a stormy night after parting from Madame, the father, son, and servant returned, surprising the baroness and Mademoiselle du Guenic, though the blind sister recognized their steps in the lane. The baron looked around the anxious circle seated by the antique lamp and said in a tremulous voice, while Gasselin replaced guns and sabres, in words of feudal simplicity: “The barons did not all do their duty.” Then he kissed his wife and sister, sat in his old arm-chair, and ordered supper for his son, Gasselin, and himself. Gasselin had thrown himself before Calyste to protect him and received a sabre cut on his shoulder; the women scarcely thanked him. The baron and guests uttered neither curses nor complaints about conquerors—silence a Breton trait. In forty years no one ever heard contumely from the baron about his adversaries; it was for them to do their duty as he did his. This silence indicates an unalterable will.

This last effort, a flash of waning energy, had caused the present weakness and somnolence of the old man. The fresh defeat and exile of the Bourbons, driven out as miraculously as restored, were a bitter sadness.

About six on the evening this history begins, the baron, who by custom dined at four, fell asleep while his wife read the Quotidienne. His head rested against the arm-chair back beside the fireplace on the garden side.

Near that gnarled ancient tree, and before the fire, the baroness sat, the type of those admirable women found most in England, Scotland, or Ireland—milk-white, golden-haired, the curls wound as if by angels, with a heavenly light in their hair. Fanny O’Brien was one of those sylphs—strong in tenderness, invincible under misfortune, soft as her voice, pure as her blue eyes; skin silken to the touch, beauty beyond painter or word. Still beautiful at forty-two, many would have counted themselves fortunate to marry her as she looked on the autumn display of flowers and fruit.

The baroness held the paper in dimpled fingers, nails cut square like an antique statue’s. Half reclining, feet out to warm, she wore a black velvet gown for the chill. The corsage rose to the throat and molded the shoulders and bosom undistorted by motherhood. Her hair was in ringlets in the English fashion, the rest twisted to the crown and pinned with a tortoiseshell comb. The color, not undecided like other blondes, gleamed like burnished gold. She braided the short nape-locks—a racial sign—concealing the tiny braid in the coif, a detail showing the care she gave her person; it was her pride to please the old baron. When a woman displays in her own home the coquetry many reserve for outside, she is a noble mother and wife; she loves without calculation, does good in secret, and worships. In Fanny the Virgin’s halo seemed to preserve her beauty from time. Her skin had taken warm pearly tones; her brow, broad and well-modeled, caught the light; turquoise eyes shone with incomparable sweetness; soft lashes and slightly sunken temples inspired mute melancholy. Her aquiline thin nose suggested high birth; pure lips wore happy smiles of inexhaustible loving-kindness. Her teeth were small and white; she had gained a slight embonpoint lately, but hips and slender waist remained pleasing. Autumn added perennial flowers to her summer beauty—nobler rounded arms, finer skin grain, fuller outlines. Above all, her open serene face and pure blue eyes expressed illimitable sympathy and angelic tenderness.

At the other hearth, in an arm-chair, the octogenarian sister, like her brother in all except dress, sat listening and knitting stockings—the work requiring no sight. Both eyes had cataracts; she refused an operation despite her sister-in-law’s entreaties. Secretly, she said it was want of courage; truly, she would not let her brother spend twenty-five louis for her. That sum saved would be for the household’s good.

These two old persons set off the baroness’ beauty. Mademoiselle Zéphirine, deprived of sight, was unaware of what eighty years had done to her face. Her pale hollow countenance, the white sightless eyes giving an appearance of death, and three or four solitary projecting teeth, framed by a little brown printed cotton hood quilted like a petticoat and trimmed with a ruche, seemed menacing. She wore a cotillon—short coarse skirt—over a quilted petticoat (a veritable mattress hiding double louis-d’or) and pockets sewn to a belt she unfastened each night and put on each morning like a garment. Her body was encased in the casaquin of Brittany, a spencer of the same cloth, with a pleated collar, the washing of which caused her only dispute with her sister-in-law—she changed it only once a week. From the large wadded sleeves came two withered yet vigorous arms, their brownish-red hands like stocking-machines eternally at work; from time to time she would thrust a long knitting needle between hood and hair to poke her white locks, thrusting it back carelessly without fear of harm. She was straight as a steeple; her erect and imposing carriage might be one of old age’s coquetries proving pride a necessary passion of life. Her smile was gay. She, too, had done her duty.

As soon as the baroness saw her husband asleep, she ceased reading. A ray of sunshine stretched across the old room, burnishing black furniture, touching ceiling carvings, dancing on time-worn chests, and brightening the old oak table; Fanny’s voice and the light made music in the octogenarian blind sister’s heart. The ray sank into the melancholy tones of twilight; the baroness fell into deep meditation, a silence noted by her sister-in-law for the last two weeks, unexplained and unasked about. Mademoiselle continued to knit; the silence deepened until the clicking of needles was plainly heard.

“You have dropped the paper, sister, but you are not asleep,” said Mademoiselle slyly.

Mariotte then entered to light the lamp, which she placed on a small table by the fire, fetched her distaff and ball of thread, and sat on a stool in the window recess to spin. Gasselin busied himself about the offices, saw to the horses, readied the stable, and fed the two hunting-dogs; their joyful barking was the last noise to awaken the echoes in the dark walls. The two dogs and two horses were the only remaining vestiges of the house’s chivalry. An imaginative man sitting on the portico steps might have quivered to the hounds’ baying and the horses’ neighing.

Gasselin was a short, thick, squat Breton, with black hair and sun-browned face, silent, slow, and obstinate as a mule, yet steady. He was forty-two and had been in the household twenty-five years, hired by Mademoiselle at fifteen on hearing of the marriage and the baron’s probable return. He considered himself part of the family, had played with Calyste, loved the horses and dogs, petted them, and wore a blue linen jacket with flapping pockets, matching waistcoat and trousers, blue stockings, and stout hob-nailed shoes; in cold he donned a goatskin.

Mariotte, also over forty, was to woman what Gasselin was to man—same complexion, figure, lively black eyes. It is curious they never married; perhaps it seemed immoral, they were so like brother and sister. Mariotte’s wages were ninety francs a year; Gasselin’s, three hundred. But thousands elsewhere would not have tempted either to leave the Guenic household. Both were under Mademoiselle’s orders, who, from the war to the baron’s return, had ruled the house. When she learned the baron would bring home a mistress, she feared losing the household sceptre, yet she was agreeably surprised to find in Fanny O’Brien a woman of high birth who disliked petty household cares and would prefer plain fare to preparing costly food for herself. Fanny was capable of all duties, strong under necessary privations but without taste for commonplace work. When the baron begged his sister, in his wife’s name, to continue in charge, the old maid kissed the baroness like a sister, made a daughter of her, adored her, and rejoiced to keep control of household management. She ran the house on an almost inconceivable economy, relaxing only for great occasions—lying-in, Calyste’s needs, and similar events.

Though the two servants were used to the stern regime and required no orders—masters’ interests were their own—Mademoiselle insisted on overseeing everything. She knew, without looking, how many nuts in the barn or oats in the bin, carrying on her belt a boatswain’s whistle to summon Mariotte with one note and Gasselin with two.

Gasselin’s greatest happiness was tending the garden, producing fine fruits and vegetables; with so little to do he would have been lost without it. After grooming horses and cleaning the ground-floor rooms, he weeded and watched for a field-mouse or the grub of the cockchafer, joyfully presenting his catch to his masters. He liked going to Croisic on fast-days to buy fish cheaper than at Guerande.

Thus no household was more united in interests, more bound together than this noble family devoted to duty. Masters and servants seemed made for one another. For twenty-five years there had been neither trouble nor discord. The only griefs were the boy’s petty ailments, the only terrors the events of 1814 and 1830. If the same tasks were performed at the same hours, and food obeyed seasons and times, this monotony, like nature’s, varied only by cloud and sun, and was sustained by affection in all hearts—the more fruitful because it sprang from natural causes.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When night had fairly fallen, Gasselin entered the hall and asked his master respectfully if he was still needed.

“You can go out, or go to bed, after prayers,” replied the baron, waking, “unless Madame or my sister—”

The two ladies signaled assent. Gasselin knelt as his masters rose to kneel on their chairs; Mariotte also knelt before her stool. Mademoiselle du Guenic then said the prayer aloud. After it was over, someone rapped at the door on the lane and Gasselin opened it.

“I dare say it is Monsieur le cure; he usually comes first,” said Mariotte.

Everyone recognized the rector’s foot on the portico steps. He bowed respectfully to the three occupants and addressed them with the unctuous civility of a priest. To the somewhat absent-minded greeting of the mistress of the house he replied with ecclesiastical inquiry.

“Are you anxious or ill, Madame la baronne?” he asked.

“Thank you, no,” she replied.

Monsieur Grimont, about fifty, of middle height, lost in his cassock from which issued two stout shoes, showed a plump visage and generally pale skin with yellow spots. His dimpled hands and placid abbatial face suggested a Dutch burgomaster; his straight black hair and lively brown eyes recalled a Breton peasant. His gaiety, that of a man whose conscience is calm, admitted a joke. He lacked the unease of rectors whose power is contested; seen through Guerande, even the most irreligious traveler would recognize him as the sovereign of that Catholic town. Yet before the du Guenics he lowered his spiritual superiority; in their salon he was a chaplain in his seigneur’s house. In church his benediction always favored the Guenic chapel, where their mailed hand and device adorned the keystone.

“I thought Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel had already arrived,” said the rector, sitting and taking the baroness’s hand to kiss it. “She is getting unpunctual. Can it be that dissipation is contagious? I see Monsieur le chevalier is again at Les Touches this evening.”

“Don’t say anything about those visits before Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel,” cried the old maid eagerly.

“Ah! mademoiselle,” remarked Mariotte, “you can’t prevent the town from gossiping.”

“What do they say?” asked the baroness.

“The young girls and the old women all say he is in love with Mademoiselle des Touches.”

“A lad of Calyste’s make is playing his proper part in making the women love him,” said the baron.

“Here comes Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel,” said Mariotte.

The gravel in the courtyard crackled under discreet footsteps; a page with a lantern accompanied the coming lady. Seeing him, Mariotte moved her stool to the great hall to talk with him by the lantern’s gleam, economizing the hall’s single oribus candle.

This elderly demoiselle was thin, dried-up, yellow as parchment, wrinkled, with gray eyes, prominent teeth, and the hands of a man. Short, slightly crooked, possibly hump-backed, she stirred a great mass of petticoats when reaching a pocket; a loud jingling of keys and money echoed among her garments. She wore, dangling from one side, the keys of a good housekeeper, and from the other a silver snuff-box, thimble, knitting-needles, and other resonant implements. Instead of Zephirine’s wadded hood she had a green bonnet—once fresh, now yellowing—of a fashion preserved by her nieces and renewed every five years at Nantes. Her gowns, too, were made by her nieces to an immutable pattern. She used the old cane with its short hook and bore the ermine of ancient Breton dukes. In her and her sister the illustrious house of the Pen-Hoels ended. Her younger sister had married a Kergarouet who, despite disapproval, added Pen-Hoel to his name and called himself Vicomte de Kergarouet-Pen-Hoel.

“Heaven has punished him,” said the old lady; “he has nothing but daughters, and the Kergarouet-Pen-Hoel name will be wiped out.”

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel had about seven thousand francs a year from rents. She had come into her property at thirty-six and managed it herself, inspecting it on horseback and displaying the firmness often seen in deformed persons. Her avarice was admired locally; she kept one woman-servant and the page, and her yearly expenses, excluding taxes, did not exceed a thousand francs. Thus she attracted the caresses of the Kergarouet-Pen-Hoels, who spent winters at Nantes and summers on the Loire estate. She was supposed ready to leave her fortune to whichever niece pleased her best; every three months one of the four demoiselles de Kergarouet-Pen-Hoel (the youngest twelve, the eldest twenty) came to spend a few days with her.

A friend of Zephirine du Guenic, Jacqueline de Pen-Hoel, devoted to the Breton grandeur of the du Guenics, had planned since Calyste’s birth to transmit her property to the chevalier by marrying him to whichever niece the Vicomtesse de Kergarouet-Pen-Hoel would bestow. She dreamed of buying back some of the best Guenic property from the farmer engagistes. When avarice has an object it ceases to be a vice; it becomes a means of virtue. Perhaps Zephirine knew Jacqueline’s plan. Perhaps even the baroness, whose soul was occupied by love for her son and tenderness for his father, guessed it, seeing with what wily perseverance Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel brought her favorite niece, Charlotte de Kergarouet, now sixteen. The rector, Monsieur Grimont, was certainly in her confidence; he helped the old maid invest her savings.

But even had Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel possessed three hundred thousand francs in gold, the du Guenics would never have shown any attention that could be construed as seeking her fortune. From true Breton pride, Jacqueline, glad of the supremacy accorded to Zephirine and the du Guenics, always showed herself honored by her relations with Madame du Guenic and her sister-in-law. She even concealed the sacrifice she made each evening in allowing her page to burn in the Guenic hall that singular gingerbread-colored candle called an oribus still used in western France.

Thus this rich old maid was nobility, pride, and grandeur personified. At the moment of this narrative, the Abbe Grimont has revealed that on the evening when the old baron, the young chevalier, and Gasselin secretly departed to join MADAME (to the terror of the baroness and the great joy of all Bretons) Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel had given the baron ten thousand francs in gold—an immense sacrifice—to which the abbe added another ten thousand, a tithe collected by him—charging the old hero to offer the whole, in the name of the Pen-Hoels and of the parish of Guerande, to the mother of Henri V.

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel treated Calyste as if her intentions gave her rights over him; her plans seemed to authorize supervision. Not strict in gallantry—she had the indulgence of old women of the old school—she held in horror modern ways. Calyste might increase her esteem by a few Breton adventures but would lose it considerably if she saw him entangled in what she called innovations. She might disinter a little gold to pay for the results of a love-affair, but if Calyste drove a tilbury or spoke of a visit to Paris she would deem him dissipated and a spendthrift. To her, novel ideas meant the overthrow of crop succession, ruin under the name of improvements, mortgaged lands as the inevitable result of experiments. Prudence was the true method of making fortune; good management meant filling granaries and waiting for a rise rather than risking experiments. By singular chance she often made lucky sales that confirmed her principles. She was thought maliciously clever but was not quick-witted; methodical as a Dutchman, prudent as a cat, and persistent as a priest, those qualities in routine-bound Brittany were practically equivalent to intellect.

“Will Monsieur du Halga join us this evening?” asked Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, taking off her knitted mittens after the usual exchange of greetings.

“Yes, mademoiselle; I met him taking his dog to walk on the mall,” replied the rector.

“Ha! then our mouche will be lively to-night. Last evening we were only four.”

At the word mouche the rector rose and took from a drawer a small round osier basket, a pile of ivory counters yellow with use, and a greasy pack of cards. He arranged counters before each player and set the basket beside the lamp with infantine eagerness and the manner of a man accustomed to this service.

A firm knock at the outer gate echoed through the mansion. The page opened, and presently the long, lean, methodically clothed Chevalier du Halga, former flag-captain to Admiral de Kergarouet, outlined himself in black on the portico.

“Welcome, chevalier!” cried Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel.

“The altar is raised,” said the abbe.

The chevalier was a man in poor health, who wore flannel for his rheumatism, a black-silk skull-cap to protect his head from fog, and a spencer to guard his chest from sudden gusts. He always carried a gold-headed cane and a little bitch named Thisbe. Fussy as a fine lady and speaking low to spare his voice, he had in earlier days been among the most intrepid officers of the old navy, winning the confidence of de Suffren and the friendship of the Comte de Portenduere. His splendid conduct as flag-captain to Admiral Kergarouet was written on his scarred face. Now no one would imagine the voice that ruled storms and the eye that compassed the sea.

The chevalier never smoked, never swore; he was gentle and tranquil as a girl, as concerned about Thisbe’s caprices as an elderly dowager. He moved as an invalid, walking as if stepping on eggs, complaining of wind, sun, or mist, yet showed the whitest teeth in the reddest gums—a fact reassuring as to his maladies, which were rather expensive, consisting of four daily meals of monastic amplitude. His frame, like the baron’s, was bony and indestructibly strong, covered with a parchment-like skin retaining the tawny color from India, whence, however, he did not bring back facts or ideas. He had emigrated with his friends, lost his property, and now ended his days with the cross of Saint-Louis and a pension of two thousand francs from the Invalides de la Marine. The slight hypochondria that made him invent imaginary ills is explained by actual suffering during the emigration. He served in the Russian navy until Emperor Alexander ordered him employed against France; he then resigned and lived at Odessa near the Duc de Richelieu, with whom he returned to France. The duke obtained for this relic of the old Breton navy the pension that enabled him to live. On the death of Louis XVIII he returned to Guerande and eventually became mayor of the city.

For the last fifteen years the rector, the chevalier, and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel had regularly passed their evenings at the hotel de Guenic, where other noble neighbors also came. It will be readily understood that the du Guenics were at the head of the faubourg Saint-Germain of the old Breton province, where no member of the new administration sent down by the government was ever allowed to penetrate. For the last six years the rector coughed when coming to the crucial words, Domine, salvum fac regem. Politics in Guerande remained at that point.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
IV. A NORMAL EVENING

Mouche is played with five cards to each player and one turned up to show trumps. Each round a player may play or abstain; abstaining costs only his stake unless there are forfeits in the basket. If he plays and wins a trick he is paid pro rata to the stake; win none and he is mouche, paying the whole stake and swelling the next basket. Those who decline throw down their cards; their play is null. Players may exchange cards with the remainder of the pack in sequence, so the first two sometimes take most of it. The turned-up trump belongs to the dealer, who is last and may exchange it for any card in his hand. One powerful card, the knave of clubs, is called Mistigris.

The game provokes cupidity, diplomacy, and poker faces. At the hotel du Guenic each player took twenty counters representing five sous, making the stake five farthings—a significant sum for this company. In extraordinary luck fifty sous could be won—more than anyone in Guerande spent in a day. Consequently Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel played mouche with the passion of a big-game hunter. Mademoiselle Zephirine, who shared with the baroness, attached equal importance, treating one farthing for a chance at five as a serious investment.

An 1825 convention stated the game should cease when anyone losing ten sous wished to retire; politeness forbade continuing without him. The chevalier and the baron, however, found a way around this: when all but one wished to continue, du Halga, a rich free-spending sailor, would offer ten counters to Zephirine or Jacqueline, recompensing them if they won. The baron made similar offers under the pretext of continuing the game. The misers accepted, though not without pressing—a maidens' custom—but only after the baron and chevalier had won, or the offer would insult them.

Mouche became brilliant when a Demoiselle de Kergarouet passed through with her aunt. The Kergarouets insisted on being called simply Kergarouet. The aunt treated the niece to mouche at du Guenic and ordered her to look amiable—easy in Calyste’s presence, whom the four Kergarouet girls adored. Brought up in modern ways, they cared little for five sous; stakes rose. These evenings excited the old blind sister, who signaled stakes to the baroness by pressing her foot a number of times. To play or not when the basket was full provoked inward struggle—cupidity versus fear. If Charlotte de Kergarouet was lucky, her aunt would nevertheless lecture her on reserve and modesty for appearing too decisive.

These nightly games, carried on for twenty years, were sometimes interrupted by gossip or political discussion. Players often sat half an hour talking with cards fanned on their stomachs. If a counter was missing from the basket, everyone declared they had put in their share. Usually the chevalier made up the deficiency and was accused of being absent-minded about his health; when he paid, Zephirine and Jacqueline fretted they themselves might have forgotten. The company enjoyed the small suspicions. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel would almost always accuse the rector of cheating when he won the basket.

“It is singular,” he replied, “that I never cheat except when I win the trick.”

Sometimes talk of royal misfortunes would distract the baron, and evenings could end without gain or loss but with emotion and complaints that mouche was dull. Once, after the Kergarouets arrived, they tried whist and boston at the baroness’s urging but could not understand them; on departure the new games were voted head-splitters and the company returned to mouche—tradition triumphing in Brittany.

While the rector dealt, the baroness asked the Chevalier du Halga the same health questions as the night before. The chevalier made it a point to invent new ailments; he never complained of real wounds but of fancied pains—the doctors have found no remedy for illnesses that don't exist. “Yesterday the trouble was, I believe, in your legs,” said the rector. “It moves about,” said the chevalier. The exchange, with light banter from Zephirine and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, displayed the chevalier’s old wit and a life rich in memories.

The conversation turned to the death of the Duc de Lenoncourt, and the baron remarked he would be next; his wife gently rebuked him. The company noticed the baron’s melancholy and afterward would say, “Monsieur du Guenic was sad to-night.” The phrase “The Baron du Guenic fails” opened many conversations.

“How is Thisbe?” asked Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel. “The poor thing is like her master,” the chevalier replied, mimicking the dog and exposing his hand—an attempt by a neighbor to see if he had Mistigris or the trump. The baroness observed the rector’s nose grow pale—his sign of holding Mistigris—because of the pleasure that card gave him.

“You had company today,” said the chevalier. “Yes, a cousin of my brother-in-law,” she replied; she told of the marriage of the Comtesse de Kergarouet, a Demoiselle de Fontaine. The chevalier, who knew the admiral, recognized the name. She then astonished them with tales about Mademoiselle des Touches—so strange she could hardly believe them; if true, Calyste would not be so constantly with her. The baron pricked up his ears.

But Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel suddenly had Mistigris and could not continue. “Your play, monsieur le baron,” she said. Zephirine returned to knitting; the rector, informed about Calyste and Mademoiselle des Touches, refrained. The baron inquired again: “What is so extraordinary?” “She smokes,” Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel said. The chevalier commented dryly. Asked about her property, the old maid said she was squandering it.

“The game is mine!” cried the baroness; she had a long run of trumps and Mistigris. The victory horrified Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, who forgot Calyste and Mademoiselle des Touches for a moment. By nine only the baroness and rector remained; the others had gone to bed. The chevalier, as usual, accompanied Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel home, commenting on Zephirine’s delight in engulfing her gains. He had also noticed the baroness’ abstraction.

At Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel’s door she confided the cause: “Calyste is lost unless we marry him promptly. He loves Mademoiselle des Touches, an actress!” “Then send for Charlotte.” “I have; my sister will receive my letter tomorrow,” she replied.

From this sketch of a normal evening one may imagine the hubbub excited throughout Guerande by any stranger’s arrival, stay, or passage.

When silence came from the baron’s and his sister’s rooms, the baroness looked at the rector, who toyed pensively with the counters. “I see that you begin to share my anxiety about Calyste,” she said. “Did you notice Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel’s displeased looks to-night?” he asked. She had the best intentions for Calyste; she loved him as though he were her son. His conduct in the Vendée beside his father and Madame's praises of his devotion had increased that affection. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel intends to execute a deed of gift leaving her property to whichever niece Calyste marries. The rector acknowledged the mother’s hope for a richer Irish match but said it was wise to have two strings to the bow—Pen-Hoel’s savings and well-managed land were not to be despised. He then described Mademoiselle des Touches’ life: a stage-woman and writer, frequenter of theatres and artists, squandering her fortune on pamphleteers, painters, and musicians, under the false name Camille Maupin. She had turned Les Touches into an improper paradise of dissipation; more wine was drunk there in weeks than in all Guerande in a year. Last year the Demoiselles Bougniol let lodgings to men suspected of being Blues; they sang wicked songs and spent time at Les Touches. And this is whom Calyste adores; he would gallop to Nantes for an infamous book but not for the Church. She is not a royalist; if Calyste were called to fight for the cause and she wanted to keep him, the chevalier might let his own father go without him.

“Oh, no!” exclaimed the baroness. The rector feared to test Calyste; the mother would suffer too much. “I will see Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel tomorrow,” he promised. “Don’t be too uneasy. The harm may not be as great as they say. I will find out the truth. Jacqueline trusts me. Calyste is our child and pupil—he will not let the devil inveigle him nor disturb his family’s plans. One fault is not vice.”

“You are only informing me of details,” said the baroness. “Was I not the first to notice the change in my Calyste? A mother keenly feels being second in her son’s heart. This crisis is a trial of motherhood. I had hoped Calyste would love someone noble and beautiful—not a stage-player, a masquerader, an author who feigns sentiments, a creature who will deceive and make him unhappy! She has had adventures—”

“With several men,” said the rector. “Yet this impious creature was born in Brittany! She dishonors her land. I shall preach upon her next Sunday.”

“Don’t do that!” cried the baroness. “The peasants and paludiers might rush to Les Touches. Calyste is Breton; something dreadful might happen if he defended her as he would the Blessed Virgin.”

“It is now ten o’clock; I must bid you good-night,” said the abbe, lighting his lantern. “Who could have told me that a young man brought up by you, trained by me to Christian ideas, a fervent Catholic, a child who has lived like a spotless lamb, would plunge into such mire?”

“But is it certain?” asked the mother. “How could any woman help loving Calyste?”

“What proof other than her staying on at Les Touches? In twenty-four years since coming of age she has never stayed so long; her visits were formerly few and short.”

“A woman over forty!” exclaimed the baroness. “In Ireland they say such a woman is the most dangerous mistress for a young man.”

“As to that I have no knowledge,” replied the rector, “and shall die in ignorance.”

“And I, too, alas!” said the baroness. “I wish I had loved deeply, so I might counsel and comfort Calyste.”

The rector did not cross the little court-yard alone; the baroness accompanied him to the gate, hoping to hear Calyste’s step. They heard the rector’s cautious tread grow fainter and the parsonage door close behind him.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
V. CALYSTE

The poor mother returned to the salon deeply distressed to find that
the whole town was aware of what she had thought known to her alone.
She sat down, trimmed the wick of the lamp with old scissors, took up
her worsted-work, and awaited Calyste. The baroness hoped to induce her
son to come home earlier and spend less time with Mademoiselle des
Touches. Such maternal calculations were vain. Day after day, Calyste’s
visits to Les Touches became more frequent, and every night he came in
later. The night before the day of which we speak it was midnight when he
returned.

The baroness, lost in maternal meditation, set her stitches with the
rapidity of one absorbed in thought while engaged in manual labor.
Whoever had seen her bending to the lamp beneath the centennial hangings
would have admired the sublimity of the picture. Fanny’s skin was so
transparent one read the thoughts that crossed her brow. Piqued with a
curiosity that often comes to a pure woman, she asked what devilish
secrets these daughters of Baal possessed to charm men so as to make
them forget mother, family, country, and self-interest. Sometimes she
longed to meet this woman and judge her soberly. Her mind measured the
evils the innovative spirit of the age—described to her as so dangerous
for young souls by the rector—would have upon her only child, until
then so guileless; as pure as an innocent girl, and beautiful with the
same fresh beauty.

Calyste, that splendid offspring of the oldest Breton race and the
noblest Irish blood, had been nurtured by his mother with the utmost
care. Until she left his training to the rector of Guerande, she was
certain no impure word or evil thought had sullied her precious son.
After nursing him at her bosom, giving him her life twice, as it were,
and guiding his footsteps as a child, she entrusted him, still innocent,
to the pastor, who, out of reverence for the family, promised a thorough
Christian education. Calyste thenceforth received the instruction the
abbé himself had had at the Seminary. The baroness taught him English,
and a teacher of mathematics was found among the employees at
Saint-Nazaire. Calyste was thus ignorant of modern literature and the
current advance of the sciences. His education had been limited to
geography, circumspect history, the Latin and Greek of seminaries, the
literature of dead languages, and a restricted choice of French writers.
When, at sixteen, he began what the Abbé Grimont called his philosophy,
he was essentially the same as when Fanny placed him in the abbé’s
hands. The Church had proved as maternal as the mother. Without being
over-pious or ridiculous, the idolized young lad was a fervent Catholic.

For this noble, innocent son the baroness desired a quiet happy life.
She expected to inherit some property, two or three thousand pounds
sterling, from an aunt. This sum, joined to the small present fortune of
the Guenics, might enable her to find a wife for Calyste who would bring
twelve or fifteen thousand francs a year. Charlotte de Kergarouet, with
her aunt’s fortune, a rich Irish girl, or any other good heiress would
have suited the baroness, who seemed indifferent as to choice. She was
ignorant of love, having never known it, and like those about her saw
marriage only as a means of fortune. Passion was unknown to these
Catholic souls, these old people exclusively concerned with salvation,
God, the king, and property. No one should be surprised, therefore, at
the foreboding thoughts that accompanied the mother’s wounded feelings,
who lived as much for her son’s future interests as for her love. If
the household would listen to wisdom, she thought, the coming Guenic
generation, by enduring privations and saving as provincials do, would
buy back their estates and recover wealth. The baroness prayed for a
long life to see this dawn. Mademoiselle du Guenic shared this hope,
which Mademoiselle des Touches now threatened.

The baroness heard midnight strike with tears; the clock struck one, and
Calyste was still not home.

“Will he stay there?” she thought. “It would be the first time. Poor
child!”

At that moment Calyste’s step resounded in the lane. Joy drove out
anxiety; the poor mother flew to the gate and opened it.

“Oh!” cried Calyste, in a grieved voice, “my darling mother, why did you
sit up for me? I have a pass-key and the tinder-box.”

“You know very well, my child, that I cannot sleep when you are out,”
she said, kissing him.

In the salon she looked at her son to discover, if possible, from his
expression the events of the evening. But he caused her, as usual, an
emotion that frequency never weakened—a feeling all loving mothers have
at sight of a masterpiece they made. Except for the black eyes, full of
energy and sun-heat which he took from his father, Calyste otherwise
resembling his mother; he had her golden hair, lovable mouth, curving
fingers, and soft white skin. Though slightly resembling a girl
disguised as a man, his physical strength was Herculean. Muscles had
the suppleness and vigor of steel springs; the singularity of his black
eyes and fair complexion was charming. His beard had not yet sprouted;
this delay is said to promise longevity. The chevalier wore a short
black velvet coat like his mother’s gown, silver buttons, a blue foulard
necktie, gray jean trousers, and gaiters. His white brow bore signs of
fatigue caused, to an observer, by painful thoughts; but his mother,
unable to suppose troubles could wring his heart, attributed weariness
to passing excitement. Calyste was handsome as a Greek god and without
conceit; he had his mother’s beauty constantly before him and cared
little for personal advantages he found useless.

“Those beautiful pure cheeks,” thought his mother, “where young blood
flows, belong to another woman! She is the mistress of that innocent
brow! Ah! passion will lead to many evils; it will tarnish those eyes,
moist as an infant’s!”

This bitter thought wrung Fanny’s heart and destroyed her pleasure.

It may seem strange that in a family of six living on three thousand
francs a year the son should have a coat and the mother a gown of
velvet; but Fanny O’Brien had aunts and wealthy relations in London who
remembered her with gifts. Several sisters, married to wealth, took
interest in Calyste, wishing to find him an heiress, knowing he was
noble and handsome.

“You stayed at Les Touches longer than last night, my dear one,” said
the mother at last, in an agitated tone.

“Yes, dear mother,” he answered, offering no explanation.

The curtness of this answer clouded her brow; she postponed the
explanation till the morrow. Mothers who admit such anxieties tremble
before their sons; they perceive what love is about to take from them,
but they feel joy in their sons’ happiness. Conflicting feelings battled
in their hearts. Though the result may be their sons’ superior growth,
true mothers dislike forced abdication; they would rather keep their
children small and needing protection.

“You are tired, dear child; go to bed,” she said, repressing her tears.

A mother ignorant of all her son’s doings thinks the worst—that is, if
the mother loves as Fanny did. The patient care of twenty years might be
ruined. This human masterpiece of virtuous and religious education might
be destroyed; the happiness prepared for his life might be ruined by
this woman.

The next day Calyste slept till mid-day; his mother would not have him
wakened. Mariotte served breakfast in his bed. The inflexible
semi-conventual rules regulating meal hours yielded to the chevalier’s
caprices. If it became necessary to borrow a key from Mademoiselle du
Guenic to obtain food outside meal hours, it was done by pretext of a
fancy of Calyste.

About one o’clock the baron, his wife, and Mademoiselle sat in the
salon, for they dined at three. The baroness read the Quotidienne to her
husband. As she finished a paragraph she heard Calyste’s steps on the
upper floor and dropped the paper.

“Calyste must be going to dine again at Les Touches; he has dressed
himself.”

“He amuses himself, the dear boy,” said the old sister, taking a
silver whistle from her pocket and whistling once.

Mariotte appeared at the communicating door.

“What is it?” she said; “anything wanted?”

“The chevalier dines at Les Touches; don’t cook the fish.”

“But we are not sure as yet,” said the baroness.

“You seem annoyed, sister; I know it by your voice.”

“Monsieur Grimont has heard grave charges against Mademoiselle des
Touches, who for the last year has so changed our dear Calyste.”

“Changed him, how?” asked the baron.

“He reads all sorts of books.”

“Ah! ah!” exclaimed the baron, “so that’s why he has given up hunting
and riding.”

“Her morals are very reprehensible, and she has taken a man’s name,”
added Madame du Guenic.

“A war name, I suppose,” said the old man. “I was called ‘l’Intime,’ the
Comte de Fontaine ‘Grand-Jacques,’ the Marquis de Montauran the ‘Gars.’
I was the friend of Ferdinand, who never submitted, any more than I did.
Ah! those were the good times; people shot each other, but what of that?
we amused ourselves.”

This war memory, pushing aside paternal anxiety, saddened Fanny for a
moment. The rector’s revelations and Calyste’s want of confidence had
kept her from sleeping.

“Suppose Monsieur le chevalier does love Mademoiselle des Touches,
where’s the harm?” said Mariotte. “She has thirty thousand francs a year
and she is very handsome.”

“What is that you say, Mariotte?” exclaimed the old baron. “A Guenic
marry a des Touches! The des Touches were not even grooms in the days
when du Guesclin considered our alliance an honor.”

“A woman who takes a man’s name—Camille Maupin!” said the baroness.

“The Maupins are an old family,” said the baron; “they bear: gules,
three—” He stopped. “But she cannot be a Maupin and a des Touches both,”
he added.

“She is called Maupin on the stage.”

“A des Touches could hardly be an actress,” said the old man. “Really,
Fanny, if I did not know you, I should think you out of your head.”

“She writes plays and books,” continued the baroness.

“Books?” said the baron, looking surprised. “I have heard that
Mademoiselle Scudery and Madame de Sévigné wrote books, but it was not
the best thing they did.”

“Are you going to dine at Les Touches, monsieur?” said Mariotte, when
Calyste entered.

“Probably,” replied the young man.

Mariotte, not inquisitive, left without waiting to hear what the
baroness would say to her son.

“Are you going again to Les Touches, my Calyste?” The baroness
emphasized my. “Les Touches is not respectable. Its mistress leads an
irregular life; she will corrupt our Calyste. Already Camille Maupin
has made him read many books; he has had adventures—You knew all that,
my naughty child, and you never said one word to your best friends!”

“The chevalier is discreet,” said his father, “a virtue of the olden
time.”

“Too discreet,” said the jealous mother, observing the red flush on her
son’s forehead.

“My dear mother,” said Calyste, kneeling beside the baroness, “I didn’t
think it necessary to publish my defeat. Mademoiselle des Touches, or,
if you choose, Camille Maupin, rejected my love more than eighteen
months ago, during her last stay at Les Touches. She laughed at me,
gently; saying she might very well be my mother; that a woman of forty
committed a sort of crime against nature in loving a minor, and that she
was incapable of such depravity. She made a thousand little jokes, which
hurt me—for she is witty as an angel; but when she saw me weep she tried
to comfort me, and offered me her friendship nobly. She has more heart
than talent; she is as generous as you are. I am now her child. On her
return here lately, hearing she loves another, I have resigned myself.
Do not repeat the calumnies against her. Camille is an artist, she has
genius, she leads one of those exceptional lives not to be judged like
ordinary lives.”

“My child,” said religious Fanny, “nothing can excuse a woman for not
conducting herself as the Church requires. She fails in her duty to God
and society by abjuring the gentle tenets of her sex. A woman sins in
even going to a theatre; but to write the impieties that actors repeat,
to roam the world, first with an enemy to the Pope, and then with a
musician, ah! Calyste, you can never persuade me such acts are deeds of
faith, hope, or charity. Her fortune was given by God to do good, and
what good does she do with hers?”

Calyste sprang up and looked at his mother.

“Mother,” he said, “Camille is my friend; I cannot hear her spoken of
this way; I would give my very life for her.”

“Your life!” said the baroness, startled. “Your life is our life, the
life of all of us.”

“My nephew has just said many things I do not understand,” said the old
sister, turning to her dozing brother.

“Where did he learn them?” said the mother; “at Les Touches.”

“Yes, my darling mother; she found me ignorant as a carp, and she has
taught me.”

“You knew the essential things when you learned the duties taught us by
religion,” replied the baroness. “Ah! this woman is fated to destroy
your noble and sacred beliefs.”

The old maid rose and solemnly stretched forth her hands toward her
brother, who woke with a start at the sound of his wife’s voice.

“Calyste,” she said, in a voice from her heart, “your father has never
opened books; he speaks Breton, he fought for God and for the king.
Educated people did the evil—be educated if you choose!”

So saying, she sat down and began to knit with a rapidity betraying
inward emotion.

“My angel,” said the mother, weeping, “I foresee some evil coming upon
you in that house.”

“Who is making Fanny weep?” cried the old man, waking. He looked round
upon his sister, his son, and the baroness. “What is the matter?” he
asked.

“Nothing, my friend,” replied his wife.

“Mamma,” said Calyste, whispering, “it is impossible for me to explain
now; but to-night you and I will talk. When you know all, you will bless
Mademoiselle des Touches.”

“Mothers do not like to curse,” replied the baroness. “I could not curse
a woman who truly loved my Calyste.”

The young man bade adieu and went out. The baron and his wife rose to
see him pass through the courtyard, open the gate, and disappear. The
baroness did not take up the newspaper; she was too agitated. In this
tranquil life such a discussion was the equivalent of a quarrel. Though
somewhat calmed, her motherly uneasiness persisted. Whither would such a
friendship lead? Bless Mademoiselle des Touches? how could that be? These
questions were as momentous to her simple soul as revolution is to a
statesman. Camille Maupin was Revolution itself in that calm home.

“I fear that woman will ruin him,” she said, picking up the paper.

“My dear Fanny,” said the old baron, with a jaunty air, “you are too
much of an angel to understand these things. Mademoiselle des Touches is,
they say, as black as a crow, as strong as a Turk, and forty years old.
Our dear Calyste was certain to fall in love with her. Of course he will
tell honorable little lies to conceal his happiness. Let him alone to
amuse himself with his first illusions.”

“If it had been any other woman—” began the baroness.

“But, my dear Fanny, if the woman were a saint she would not accept your
son.” The baroness picked up the paper. “I will go and see her myself,”
added the baron, “and tell you all about her.”

This speech has no savor at the moment. But after reading the biography
of Camille Maupin you can imagine the old baron entering the lists
against that illustrious woman.

VI. BIOGRAPHY OF CAMILLE MAUPIN

The town of Guerande, which for two months past had seen Calyste, its
flower and pride, often going to Les Touches, concluded Mademoiselle
Félicité des Touches was passionately in love with the youth, and that
she practised all kinds of sorceries. Young girls and wives asked by what
right an old woman exercised so absolute an empire over that angel. When
Calyste passed along the Grand Rue many a regretful eye watched him.

It became necessary to explain the rumors about the person Calyste went
to see. These rumors, swollen by Breton gossip, reached the rector.
The receiver of taxes, the juge de paix, the head of Saint-Nazaire
custom-house and other lettered persons did not reassure the abbé by
relating the strange life of the female writer who concealed herself
under the masculine name Camille Maupin. She did not yet eat little
children nor kill lovers like Cleopatra, but to Abbé Grimont this
monstrous creature—a cross between a siren and an atheist—violated
every social law invented to restrain the infirmities of womankind.

Just as Clara Gazul is the female pseudonym of a distinguished male
writer, George Sand the masculine pseudonym of a gifted woman, so Camille
Maupin was the mask behind a charming Breton, Félicité des Touches, who
was now causing lively anxiety to the Baronne du Guenic and the
excellent rector of Guerande. The Breton des Touches family has no
connection with the Touraine family of that name.

Camille Maupin, one of the few celebrated women of the nineteenth
century, was long supposed to be a man because of the virility of her
first writings. All the world now knows the two volumes of plays,
published in 1822, that made a literary revolution when the question of
the classics and the romanticists palpitated. Since then, Camille Maupin
has written several plays and a novel, which sustained her initial
success. To explain why Félicité des Touches became a man and an author,
and why she kept her freedom and thus was more excusable than Madame de
Staël, would satisfy curiosity and do justice to a rare figure. Though
she plays a secondary role here, her importance in literary history and
influence on Calyste justify pausing before her.

Mademoiselle Félicité des Touches became an orphan in 1793. Her
property escaped confiscation by reason of her father’s and brother’s
deaths. Her father, killed on 10 August among defenders of the king,
served as major of the guards; her brother was massacred at Les Carmes.
