“Why,” said Felicite, as if to break up the discussion, “do young men like my Calyste, begin by loving women of a certain age?”

“I don’t know any sentiment more artless or more generous,” replied Vignon. “It is the natural consequence of the adorable qualities of youth. Besides, how would old women end if it were not for such love? You are young and beautiful, and will be for twenty years to come, so I can speak of this matter before you,” he added, with a keen look at Mademoiselle des Touches. “In the first place the semi-dowagers, to whom young men pay their first court, know much better how to make love than younger women. An adolescent youth is too like a young woman himself for a young woman to please him. Such a passion trenches on the fable of Narcissus. Besides that feeling of repugnance, there is, as I think, a mutual sense of inexperience which separates them. The reason why the hearts of young women are only understood by mature men, who conceal their cleverness under a passion real or feigned, is precisely the same (allowing for the difference of minds) as that which renders a woman of a certain age more adroit in attracting youth. A young man feels that he is sure to succeed with her, and the vanities of the woman are flattered by his suit. Besides, isn’t it natural for youth to fling itself on fruits? The autumn of a woman’s life offers many that are very toothsome,--those looks, for instance, bold, and yet reserved, bathed with the last rays of love, so warm, so sweet; that all-wise elegance of speech, those magnificent shoulders, so nobly developed, the full and undulating outline, the dimpled hands, the hair so well arranged, so cared for, that charming nape of the neck, where all the resources of art are displayed to exhibit the contrast between the hair and the flesh-tones, and to set in full relief the exuberance of life and love. Brunettes themselves are fair at such times, with the amber colors of maturity. Besides, such women reveal in their smiles and display in their words a knowledge of the world; they know how to converse; they can call up the whole of social life to make a lover laugh; their dignity and their pride are stupendous; or, in other moods, they can utter despairing cries which touch his soul, farewells of love which they take care to render useless, and only make to intensify his passion. Their devotions are absolute; they listen to us; they love us; they catch, they cling to love as a man condemned to death clings to the veriest trifles of existence,--in short, love, absolute love, is known only through them. I think such women can never be forgotten by a man, any more than he can forget what is grand and sublime. A young woman has a thousand distractions; these women have none. No longer have they self-love, pettiness, or vanity; their love--it is the Loire at its mouth, it is vast, it is swelled by all the illusions, all the affluents of life, and this is why--but my muse is dumb,” he added, observing the ecstatic attitude of Mademoiselle des Touches, who was pressing Calyste’s hand with all her strength, perhaps to thank him for having been the occasion of such a moment, of such an eulogy, so lofty that she did not see the trap that it laid for her.

During the rest of the evening Claude Vignon and Felicite sparkled with wit and happy sayings; they told anecdotes, and described Parisian life to Calyste, who was charmed with Claude, for mind has immense seductions for persons who are all heart.

“I shouldn’t be surprised to see the Marquise de Rochefide and Conti, who, of course, will accompany her, at the landing-place to-morrow,” said Claude Vignon, as the evening ended. “When I was at Croisic this afternoon, the fishermen were saying that they had seen a little vessel, Danish, Swedish, or Norwegian, in the offing.”

This speech brought a flush to the cheeks of the impassible Camille.

Again Madame du Guenic sat up till one o’clock that night, waiting for her son, unable to imagine why he should stay so late if Mademoiselle des Touches did not love him.

“He must be in their way,” said this adorable mother. “What were you talking about?” she asked, when at last he came in.

“Oh, mother, I have never before spent such a delightful evening. Genius is a great, a sublime thing! Why didn’t you give me genius? With genius we can make our lives, we can choose among all women the woman to love, and she must be ours.”

“How handsome you are, my Calyste!”

“Claude Vignon is handsome. Men of genius have luminous foreheads and eyes, through which the lightnings flash--but I, alas! I know nothing--only to love.”

“They say that suffices, my angel,” she said, kissing him on the forehead.

“Do you believe it?”

“They say so, but I have never known it.”

Calyste kissed his mother’s hand as if it was a sacred thing.

“I will love you for all those that would have adored you,” he said.

“Dear child! perhaps it is a little bit your duty to do so, for you inherit my nature. But, Calyste, do not be unwise, imprudent; try to love only noble women, if love you must.”
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
What young man full of restrained life and emotion would not think of going to Croisic to see Madame de Rochefide land, and to examine her incognito? Calyste surprised his parents by going off in the morning without waiting for breakfast. His feet moved with an almost unknown agility; he kept close to the walls of Les Touches lest he be seen. He was ashamed of his ardor and afraid of being laughed at; Felicite and Vignon were so perspicacious! Young men fancy their foreheads transparent.

He reached the shore, strengthened by a stone embankment, in front of a shelter for when tide and weather made the crossing to Croisic impossible. Two boats came from Croisic, laden with baggage—trunks, packages, chests—whose look told a native they belonged to travellers of distinction. In one boat was a maid in a straw bonnet with a green veil and a man; it proved to be only a maid and a footman.

“Are you going over to Croisic, Monsieur Calyste?” asked a boatman; Calyste, annoyed at being called by name, shook his head.

He was captivated by a chest covered with tarred cloth labeled MME. LA MARQUISE DE ROCHEFIDE. The name shone before him like a talisman; he fancied there was something fateful in it. In the desert of his newly awakened desires his fancy fastened on the first woman presented. Beatrix thus inherited the love Camille had rejected.

As the luggage landed Calyste glanced toward Croisic for another boat that might show him Beatrix—already the marble statue on which to hang his garlands. He stood with arms folded, lost in meditation. Remarkably, we often subject ourselves to sentiments by our own volition, deliberately bind ourselves; chance has less to do with it than we think.

“Are you expecting someone to meet you?” the maid asked.

“We were told they would fetch us from Les Touches. If they don’t come,” she added, “I don’t know how Madame la marquise will manage to dress for dinner. You had better go and find Mademoiselle des Touches. Oh! what a land of savages!”

Calyste felt he had blundered.

“Is your mistress going to Les Touches?” he inquired.

“She is there; Mademoiselle came for her this morning at seven o’clock. Ah! here come the horses.”

Calyste started toward Guerande with the lightness of a chamois, doubling to avoid retracing his steps or meeting servants of Les Touches. He met two of them on the narrow causeway through the marsh.

“Shall I go in, or shall I not?” he thought when the pines of Les Touches came in sight. He was afraid, and continued to Guerande, where he finished his excursion on the mall and reflected.

“She has no idea of my agitation,” he said to himself.

His capricious thoughts were grapnels fastening his heart to the marquise. He had known none of these mysterious terrors and joys with Camille. Such vague emotions rise like poems in the untutored soul. Warmed by the first fires of imagination, souls like his pass through preparation and may reach in silence the heights of love without having met the object of their efforts.

Presently Calyste saw the Chevalier du Halga and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel walking together on the mall. He heard them say his name and slipped aside to listen. The chevalier advised Pen-Hoel, if Charlotte de Kergarouet came, to keep her four or five months so the girl might fall in love with Calyste by daily contact, allowing them to marry next winter. “If you say two words about it to Charlotte she’ll say four to Calyste,” he added; “a girl of sixteen can certainly carry off the prize from a woman of forty.”

The old people turned away and Calyste heard no more. Remembering what his mother had told him, he saw Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel’s intention. The idea of a girl thus imposed upon him sent him with greater ardor into his imaginary love. He had never fancied Charlotte; he now felt repugnance at the thought. Calyste, raised in modest means and accustomed to restrained life, cared little for fortune; his thoughts belonged to the marquise. What was that little Charlotte beside the portrait Camille had drawn—more a sister than a rival?

He did not return home until five. His mother handed him a letter from Mademoiselle des Touches:

  My dear Calyste,—The beautiful marquise has come; we count on you to help us celebrate her arrival. Claude, always sarcastic, declares that you will play Bice and that she will be Dante. It is for our honor as Bretons, and yours as a du Guenic to welcome a Casteran. Come soon.
  Your friend, Camille Maupin.
  Come as you are, without ceremony; otherwise you will put us to the blush.

Calyste gave the letter to his mother and departed.

“Who are the Casterans?” said Fanny to the baron.

“An old Norman family, allied to William the Conqueror,” he replied. “They bear on a shield tierce fessed azure, gules and sable, a horse rearing argent, shod with gold. That beautiful creature for whom the Gars was killed at Fougeres in 1800 was the daughter of a Casteran who made herself a nun and became an abbess after the Duc de Verneuil deserted her.”

“And the Rochefides?”

“I don’t know that name. I should have to see the blazon,” he replied.

The baroness was somewhat reassured that the Marquise de Rochefide was of noble birth, but she felt her son exposed to new seductions.

Calyste walked along with violent yet soft inward movements; his throat was tight, his heart swelled, his brain was full, fever possessing him. He tried to walk slowly, but some superior power hurried him. Such impetuosity is common in young men: a subtle fire makes Nature and woman seem radiant.

He found the company assembled in Camille’s little salon. It was about six; the setting sun cast a ruddy light chequered by trees; twilight, beloved of women, spread through the room.

“Here comes the future deputy of Brittany,” said Camille as Calyste raised the tapestry portiere—“punctual as a king.”

“You recognized his step just now,” said Claude to Felicite in a low voice.

Calyste bowed low to the marquise, who inclined her head; he did not look at her but took the hand Claude Vignon held out and pressed it.

“This is the celebrated man of whom we have talked so much, Gennaro Conti,” said Camille, not replying to Claude’s remark.

She presented a man of medium height, thin, with chestnut hair, eyes almost red, and a freckled white skin, whose head so resembled Lord Byron’s that description seemed superfluous. Conti was proud of the resemblance.

“I am fortunate,” he said, “to meet Monsieur du Guenic during the one day that I spend at Les Touches.”

“It was for me to say that to you,” Calyste replied with a certain ease.

“He is handsome as an angel,” said the marquise in an undertone to Felicite.

Standing between the sofa and the two ladies, Calyste heard the words. He seated himself and looked furtively at the marquise. In the soft half-light he saw, reclining on a divan, a white serpentine shape that thrilled him. Felicite had done her friend a service; the marquise was much superior to Camille’s unflattering portrait. Had Beatrix wound blue-bells into her hair to set off her creped curls? The circles at her eyes, showing fatigue, were mother-of-pearl; her skin dazzled with fine blue veins. The delicacy of her features, diaphanous forehead, and long graceful neck lent many expressions. Her waist might be spanned by the hands; bare shoulders sparkled like a white camellia. A transparent fichu revealed the bosom's outline. A white muslin gown strewn with blue flowers, large sleeves, and shoes with crossed strings over stockings showed a charming knowledge of dress. Silver filigree ear-rings harmonized with the soft curls starred with blue-bells.

Calyste’s eager eye took in these beauties at a glance and carved them on his soul. The fair Beatrix and the dark Felicite might have sat for contrasting portraits: force and feebleness of womanhood in full development. In a moment—at first sight—Calyste was seized with a love that crowned his hopes and fears. Mademoiselle des Touches had awakened his nature; Beatrix inflamed heart and thought. He felt a sudden power to conquer; he looked at Conti with an envious, gloomy rivalry he’d never felt for Claude Vignon. He strove to control himself; the inward tempest fell as soon as Beatrix’s eyes turned to him and her soft voice sounded. Dinner was announced.

“Calyste, give your arm to the marquise,” said Camille, taking Conti with her right hand and Claude Vignon with her left, drawing back to let the marquise pass.

The descent of that ancient staircase was to Calyste like going into battle. His heart failed him; he had nothing to say; a slight sweat pearled on his forehead and wet his back; his arm trembled so that as they reached the lowest step the marquise asked, “Is anything the matter?”

“Oh!” he replied muffled, “I have never seen any woman so beautiful as you, except my mother, and I am not master of my emotions.”

“But you have Camille Maupin before your eyes.”

“Ah! what a difference!” said Calyste ingenuously.

“Calyste,” whispered Felicite behind him, “did I not tell you that you would forget me as if I had never existed? Sit there,” she said aloud, “beside the marquise, on her right, and you, Claude, on her left. As for you, Gennaro, I retain you by me; we will keep a mutual eye on their coquetries.”

Camille’s peculiar accept struck Claude Vignon, who observed her closely and never ceased to examine Mademoiselle des Touches during dinner.

“Coquetries!” replied the marquise, taking off her gloves and showing her beautiful hands; “the opportunity is good, with a poet,” and she motioned to Claude, “on one side, and poesy the other.”

At these words Conti turned and gave Calyste a flattering look.

By candlelight Beatrix seemed even more beautiful. The gleam made her forehead satiny, lit the spangles of her eyes, and turned her curls to gold. She threw back her thin gauze scarf, disclosing a beautiful nape and graceful neck—unlike Camille’s, the sign of another character.

Calyste had trouble pretending to eat; nervous motions deprived him of appetite. The ardor of the heart, restrained by moral reserve, led to inward conflict—the long respectful hesitations and tender debatings of the pure-hearted. Studying furtively the marquise’s beauty, Calyste felt oppressed by her majesty; dwarfed by the hauteur of certain glances, the imposing expression of an aristocratic face, and the pride women express in slight motions. The false position she had assumed forced an imposing manner—an art of the great world fatal to vulgar women.

Felicite’s eyes made Beatrix aware of the inward adoration she inspired in the youth beside her, and that it would be unworthy to encourage it. She now and then gave him repressive glances that fell upon his heart like an avalanche of snow. The unfortunate young fellow turned to Felicite with a look in which she could read the tears he was suppressing. She asked in a friendly tone why he ate nothing; the question piqued him and he forced himself to eat and take part in the conversation.

But Madame de Rochefide paid little attention to him. Mademoiselle des Touches, speaking wittily of her journey to Italy, related many incidents that made Claude Vignon, Conti, and Felicite laugh.

“How far such a woman is from me! Will she ever deign to notice me?” thought Calyste.

Camille was struck with his expression and tried to console him with a sympathetic look. Claude intercepted and became sarcastically gay, telling Beatrix that love existed only by desire; that most women deceived themselves in loving; that they loved for reasons unknown to men and often to themselves; that the best among them were artful.

“Keep to books, and don’t criticise our lives,” said Camille, glancing imperiously at him.

The dinner ceased to be gay. Claude’s sarcasm made the two women pensive. Calyste felt pain amid the happiness of looking at Beatrix. Conti searched the marquise’s eyes to guess her thoughts. When dinner was over, Mademoiselle des Touches took Calyste’s arm, gave the other two men to the marquise, and let them pass so she could be alone with the young Breton a moment.

“My dear Calyste,” she said, “you are acting in a manner that embarrasses the marquise; she may be delighted with your admiration, but she cannot accept it. Pray control yourself.”

“She was hard to me, she will never care for me,” said Calyste, “and if she does not I shall die.”

“Die! you! My dear Calyste, you are a child. Would you have died for me?”

“You have made yourself my friend,” he answered.

After coffee, Vignon asked Conti to sing. Camille sat at the piano. Together she and Gennaro sang the duet Dunque il mio bene tu mia sarai, the last duet of Zingarelli’s Romeo e Giulietta, a most pathetic modern page. The passage Di tanti palpiti expresses love in all its grandeur. Calyste, sitting in the same arm-chair in which Felicite had told him the story of the marquise, listened in rapt devotion. Beatrix and Vignon were on either side of the piano. Conti’s sublime voice blended with Felicite’s; both had often sung this piece and used all their gift. The music became a poem of divine melancholy, the farewell of two swans to life. When it was over, all present were under feelings that defy vulgar applause.

“Ah! music is the first of arts!” exclaimed the marquise.

“Camille thinks youth and beauty the first of poesies,” said Claude Vignon.

Mademoiselle des Touches looked at Claude with vague uneasiness. Beatrix, not seeing Calyste, turned to see the music’s effect—more for Conti’s sake than Calyste’s; she saw a white face bathed in tears. At the sight, and as if seized by sudden pain, she turned back quickly and looked at Gennaro. Music had arisen before Calyste, touching him until he stood in presence of creation. He was dumbfounded by Conti’s genius. Despite Camille’s warnings about the musician, Calyste believed now in the beauty of the soul expressed by such love. How could he rival such an artist? What woman could cease to adore that genius? That voice entered the soul like another soul.

The poor lad was overwhelmed by poesy and despair. He felt himself of no account; this ingenuous admission of his nothingness was readable on his face mingled with admiration. He did not observe the gesture with which Beatrix, moved by the contagion of a true feeling, called Felicite’s attention to him.

“Oh! the adorable heart!” cried Camille. “Conti, you will never obtain applause of one-half the value of that child’s homage. Let us sing this trio. Beatrix, my dear, come.”

When the marquise, Camille, and Conti arranged themselves at the piano, Calyste rose softly, flung himself on a sofa in the bedroom, whose door stood open, and sat with his head in his hands, plunged in meditation.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
X. DRAMA

“What is it, my child?” said Claude Vignon, who had slipped into the bedroom after Calyste and now took him by the hand. “You love; you think you are disdained; but it is not so. The field will be free to you in a few days and you will reign--beloved by more than one.”

“Loved!” cried Calyste, springing up, and beckoning Claude into the library, “Who loves me here?”

“Camille,” replied Claude.

“Camille loves me? And you!--what of you?”

“I?” answered Claude, “I--” He stopped; sat down on a sofa and rested his head with weary sadness on a cushion. “I am tired of life, but I have not the courage to quit it,” he went on after a short silence. “I wish I were mistaken in what I have just told you; but for the last few days more than one vivid light has come into my mind. I did not wander about the marshes for my pleasure; no, upon my soul I did not! The bitterness of my words when I returned and found you with Camille were the result of wounded feeling. I intend to have an explanation with her soon. Two minds as clear-sighted as hers and mine cannot deceive each other. Between two such professional duellists the combat cannot last long. Therefore I may as well tell you now that I shall leave Les Touches; yes, to-morrow perhaps, with Conti. After we are gone strange things will happen here. I shall regret not witnessing conflicts of passion of a kind so rare in France, and so dramatic. You are very young to enter such dangerous lists; you interest me; were it not for the profound disgust I feel for women, I would stay and help you play this game. It is difficult; you may lose it; you have to do with two extraordinary women, and you feel too much for one to use the other judiciously. Beatrix is dogged by nature; Camille has grandeur. Probably you will be wrecked between those reefs, drawn upon them by the waves of passion. Beware!”

Calyste’s stupefaction enabled Claude to speak thus without interruption. To hear from Claude that Camille loved him, at the very moment when he felt he loved Beatrix for life, was a weight too heavy for his untried soul. Goaded by regret for the past and crushed by his position between Beatrix, whom he loved, and Camille, whom he had ceased to love, the boy sat despairing and undecided. He sought in vain the reasons for Felicite’s rejection of his love and for bringing Claude from Paris to oppose it. Now and then Beatrix’s voice from the little salon reached him, and with it a savage desire to rush in and carry her off. What must he do? Could he come to Les Touches? If Camille loved him how could he come there to adore Beatrix? No solution presented itself.

Silence reigned. Midnight sounded on the clock of the adjoining bedroom, and the voices of Claude and Camille roused him. Before he could rise, he heard Claude speak to Felicite in a voice terrible with truth.

“You came to Paris last year desperately in love with Calyste,” Claude was saying, “but you were horrified at the thought of the consequences of such a passion at your age; it would lead to a gulf, perhaps to suicide. Love cannot exist unless it thinks itself eternal, and you saw a horrible parting. You thought of ‘Adolphe.’ Then you took me, as soldiers use fascines to build a screen between themselves and the enemy. You brought me to Les Touches to mask your real feelings and leave yourself free to follow your secret adoration. The scheme was grand and ignoble both; but to carry it out you should have chosen a common man or one so preoccupied that you could easily deceive him. You thought me simple and easy to mislead as a man of genius. I am not a man of genius, I am a man of talent, and as such I have divined you. When I praised women of your age yesterday, explaining why Calyste loved you, did you suppose I took your ravished, dazzling glance to be for me? The eyes were turned on me, but the heart on Calyste. You have never been loved, my poor Maupin, and you never will be after rejecting the beautiful fruit offered you at the portals of that hell of woman, whose lock is the numeral 50!”

“Why has love fled me?” she said in a low voice. “Tell me, you who know all.”

“Because you are not lovable,” he answered. “You do not bend to love; love must bend to you. You were never naive; your mind has too much depth; your strength repulses men of strength who fear a struggle. You are grand and sublime; bear with the consequence of those two qualities--they fatigue.”

“What a sentence!” cried Camille. “Am I not a woman? Do you think me an anomaly?”

“Possibly,” said Claude.

“We will see!” said the woman, stung.

“Farewell, my dear Camille; I leave to-morrow. I am not angry; I think you the greatest of women, but if I continued to serve you as a screen,” said Claude, with emphasis, “you would despise me. We can part now without pain; we have neither happiness to regret nor hopes betrayed. To you, as to some few men of genius, love is not what Nature made it; you see it as Christianity created it--an ideal kingdom of noble sentiments and moral devotions. I thought to share that; you misled me. I return to my misery,--to my prison of Paris. Such deception would have once made me flee existence; to-day it plants a disenchantment that will plunge me into solitude. I am without the faith which helps the Fathers to people theirs with sacred images. It is to this, my dear Camille, the superiority of our mind has brought us; we may both sing that terrible hymn: ‘Lord God, Thou hast made me powerful and solitary.’”

At this moment Calyste appeared.

“I ought not to leave you ignorant that I am here,” he said.

Mademoiselle des Touches showed the utmost fear; a sudden flush colored her impassible face. During this strange scene she was more beautiful than at any other moment of her life.

“We thought you gone, Calyste,” said Claude. “But this involuntary discretion will do no harm; perhaps you may be more at your ease at Les Touches by knowing Felicite as she is. Her silence shows me I am not mistaken as to the part she meant me to play. As I told you before, she loves you, but it is for yourself, not for herself,--a sentiment few women conceive; few know the voluptuous pleasure of sufferings born of longing,--that magnificent passion reserved for man. But she is in some sense a man,” he added sardonically. “Your love for Beatrix will make her suffer and make her happy too.”

Tears came into Camille’s eyes; she could not look at Claude or Calyste. She had supposed it impossible for any man, however keen, to perceive a delicacy so self-immolating. Her evident humiliation made Calyste share her emotion. He fell at her feet and kissed her hands, laying his tearful face upon them.

“Claude,” she said, “do not abandon me, or what will become of me?”

“What have you to fear?” replied the critic. “Calyste has fallen in love at first sight with the marquise; you cannot find a better barrier between you than that. This passion of his is worth more to you than I. Yesterday there might have been danger for you and for him; to-day you can take a maternal interest in him,” he said, with a mocking smile, “and be proud of his triumphs.”

Camille looked at Calyste, who had raised his head abruptly. Claude enjoyed, for vengeance, the sight of their confusion.

“You yourself have driven him to Madame de Rochefide,” continued Claude, “and he is now under the spell. You have dug your own grave. Had you confided in me, you would have escaped the sufferings that await you.”

“Sufferings!” cried Camille, taking Calyste’s head in her hands and kissing his hair, on which her tears fell. “No, Calyste; forget what you have heard; I count for nothing in all this.”

She rose with a dominant look that subdued both men.

“While Claude was speaking,” she said, “I conceived the beauty and the grandeur of love without hope; it is the sentiment that brings us nearest God. Do not love me, Calyste; but I will love you as no woman will!”

It was the cry of a wounded eagle. Claude himself knelt, took Camille’s hand, and kissed it.

“Leave us now, Calyste,” she said, “it is late, and your mother will be uneasy.”

Calyste returned to Guerande with lagging steps, glancing again and again to see the light from Beatrix’s room. He was surprised to find how little pity he felt for Camille, but presently felt the agony of her suffering again, and imagined he heard the moans of that noble woman.

At his paternal home total silence reigned. He saw his mother sewing by the lamp; tears moistened his eyes.

“What has happened?” cried Fanny, seeing his emotion.

For answer Calyste took his mother in his arms and kissed her cheeks, forehead, and hair with passionate effusion.

“It is you I love, you!” cried Calyste; “you who live for me; you whom I long to render happy!”

“But you are not yourself, my child,” said the baroness. “What has happened to you?”

“Camille loves me, but I love her no longer,” he answered.

The next day Calyste told Gasselin to watch the road to Saint-Nazaire and report if Mademoiselle des Touches’ carriage passed. Gasselin reported it had passed with four persons.

“Then saddle my horse and my father’s.”

Gasselin departed.

“My, nephew, what mischief is in you now?” said his Aunt Zephirine.

“Let the boy amuse himself, sister,” cried the baron. “Yesterday he was dull; to-day he is gay.”

“Did you tell him that our dear Charlotte was to arrive to-day?” said Zephirine.

“No,” replied the baroness.

“I thought perhaps he was going to meet her,” said Mademoiselle du Guenic, slyly.

“If Charlotte is to stay three months with her aunt, he will have plenty of opportunities to see her,” said his mother.

“Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel wants me to marry Charlotte, to save me from perdition,” said Calyste, laughing.

“It is written above,” said the old maid; “I shall not die tranquil or happy. I wanted to see our family continued. What can you, my fine nephew, put in the scale against such duties? Is it that actress at Les Touches?”

“What?” said the baron; “how can Mademoiselle des Touches hinder Calyste’s marriage, when it becomes necessary for us to make it? I shall go and see her.”

“I assure you, father,” said Calyste, “that Felicite will never be an obstacle to my marriage.”

Gasselin appeared with the horses.

“Where are you going, chevalier?” said his father.

“To Saint-Nazaire.”

“Ha, ha! and when is the marriage to be?” said the baron, believing Calyste hurried to see Charlotte. “Spare the horses,” he continued; “they have more than thirty miles to go.”

Calyste started with a tender farewell to his mother.

“Dear treasure!” she said as he rode through the gateway.

“God keep him!” replied the baron; “for we cannot replace him.”

The words made the baroness shudder.

“My nephew does not love Charlotte enough to ride to Saint-Nazaire after her,” said the old blind woman to Mariotte.

“No; but a fine lady, a marquise, has come to Les Touches,” said Mariotte.

“They’ll kill him,” said Mademoiselle du Guenic.

“That won’t kill him,” replied Mariotte, pleased with Calyste’s behavior.

Calyste rode fast until Gasselin asked if he was trying to catch the boat. He had no wish to see Conti or Claude, but expected to be invited to ride back with the ladies. He thought to himself of the road Camille had passed and its trees.

“Tiens! here comes the coach from Nazaire,” cried Gasselin.

Calyste found Camille and the marquise waving to passengers on the departing steamer. Beatrix was charming in a rice-straw hat and muslin gown, poised like a statue. Conti could see Calyste from the vessel.

“I thought,” said Calyste, “that you would probably come back alone.”

“You have done right, Calyste,” she replied, pressing his hand.

Beatrix turned, saw her young lover, and gave him an imperious look. A smile on Camille’s lips made the marquise aware of the vulgarity of such conduct. She said to Calyste, smiling,--

“Are you not guilty of a slight impertinence in supposing that I should bore Camille, if left alone with her?”

“My dear, one man to two widows is none too much,” said Mademoiselle des Touches, taking Calyste’s arm, and leaving Beatrix to watch the vessel.

At this moment voices approached: Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, the Vicomtesse de Kergarouet, Charlotte, and Gasselin. Charlotte saw Calyste.

“Why, there’s Calyste!” she exclaimed.

“Go and offer them seats in my carriage,” said Camille to Calyste; “the maid can sit with the coachman. I saw those ladies lose their places.”

Calyste carried the message. Madame de Kergarouet was surprised the viscountess’s elder sister refused to profit by Camille’s offer. In Nantes Camille was read and admired, justified by fortune and success. The viscountess, eager to see her, dragged her old sister forward.

“Good-morning, Calyste,” said Charlotte.

“Oh! good-morning, Charlotte,” replied Calyste, not offering his arm. Both were confused. Charlotte had built a castle in Spain of romantic hopes now ruined. She and Calyste had played together in childhood; she had assumed a future with him. Now she faced jealousy.

Charlotte was of ordinary height, with a round face, black eyes, and abundant brown hair. Her travelling dress of common stuff seemed horrible beside the fresh toilettes of Beatrix and Camille. The Vicomtesse was tall, hard, withered, full of pretensions and local gossip, aiming to humiliate Parisians and fish for compliments. Her manners had passed to her daughters. Meeting Camille and Beatrix would be a future topic for a hundred conversations.

“Monsieur le chevalier,” she said to Camille and Beatrix, pointing to Calyste, “has conveyed your friendly proposal, but we fear to inconvenience you.”

“Sister, I shall not put these ladies to inconvenience,” said Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel; “I can find a horse in Saint-Nazaire.”

Camille and Beatrix exchanged a glance that annihilated Calyste’s memories and projects. “We can very well put five in the carriage,” replied Mademoiselle des Touches. The viscountess overwhelmed her with thanks.

“Good heavens, Calyste!” said Camille in his ear as he returned with Madame de Rochefide, “are you to have that for a mother-in-law, and the little one for a wife?”

“Because her aunt is rich,” replied Calyste, sarcastically.

The party moved toward the inn. The viscountess made Camille a speech on the savages of Saint-Nazaire.

“I love Brittany, madame,” replied Camille gravely. “I was born at Guerande.”

Calyste admired Mademoiselle des Touches; she put him at ease in spite of the previous night’s declarations. Her eyes were ringed with dark circles showing she had not slept, but her brow dominated the inward storm with cold placidity.

“What queens!” he said to Charlotte, offering her his arm.

“What an idea your mother has had,” said the old maid, taking Charlotte’s other arm, “to put herself in the company of that reprobate woman!”

“Oh, you!” replied Calyste, “she has bewitched you.”

“Mademoiselle Charlotte is right,” he said. “I regard her with the same friendship I feel for you.”

“Since when have the du Guenics taken to telling lies?” asked the old maid.

“Since the Pen-Hoels have grown deaf,” replied Calyste.

“Are you not in love with her?” demanded the old maid.

“I have been, but I am so no longer,” he said.

“Bad boy! then why have you given us such anxiety? I know very well that love is only foolishness; there is nothing solid but marriage,” she remarked, glancing at Charlotte.

Charlotte hoped to recover by recalling childhood memories. They leaned affectionately; Calyste resolved to have a clear explanation with the little heiress.

The horses were put in; Camille placed Madame de Kergarouet and Charlotte on the back seat. Calyste rode beside the carriage all the way; the horses went slowly enough to allow him to keep his eyes on Beatrix.

History must lose the curious conversations that went on between the four in the carriage, for it is impossible to report the hundred and more versions that went the round of Nantes. The viscountess tried to probe Camille’s authorship.

“How do you write your books?” she began.

“Much as you do your worsted-work or knitting,” replied Camille.

“But where do you find those deep reflections, those seductive pictures?”

“Where you find the witty things you say, madame; there is nothing so easy as to write books, provided you will--”

Charlotte felt her mother’s absurdity and tried to stop it; but the viscountess went on.

Calyste, trotting slowly beside the carriage, could only see the faces of the two ladies on the front seat. Beatrix constantly avoided meeting his eyes, and held her shawl and hands crossed, apparently plunged in meditation.

In a shaded part of the road, Camille called Madame de Rochefide’s attention to the harmonies of the place and praised Calyste’s riding.

“He rides like an Englishman,” said the marquise indifferently.

“His mother is Irish,--an O’Brien,” said Charlotte, thinking herself insulted.

They drove through Guerande; a crowd gathered, astonished. Calyste rode on to announce the company’s arrival; then returned to give his arm to the two ladies and bid Camille and Beatrix adieu.

He kissed Felicite’s hand, hoping to kiss the marquise’s, but she kept her arms crossed. Camille whispered a friendly touch to his ear.

“Quite true,” thought Calyste. “I am forgetting her advice--but I shall always forget it, I’m afraid.”

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel, the Vicomtesse, and Charlotte found dinner ready and were treated with cordiality by the du Guenics. The rector and the chevalier arrived in time for liqueurs. After dinner there was an enthusiastic cry for mouche. All supposed Calyste free and almost married to Charlotte. He alone kept silence, comparing his life-long friends with the two Parisians, witty, accomplished, and tasteful. He could find no excuse to leave to go to Les Touches, when Madame de Kergarouet mentioned she regretted not accepting Camille’s carriage offer for the return to Saint-Nazaire.

Fanny saw her son’s uneasiness and intervened.

“Madame,” she said to the viscountess, “you would do better to accept Mademoiselle des Touches’ offer. Calyste, go up to Les Touches and arrange it; but don’t be long.”

“It won’t take me ten minutes,” cried Calyste, kissing his mother.

XI. FEMALE DIPLOMACY

Calyste ran to Les Touches and reached the portico just as Camille and Beatrix were leaving the grand salon after dinner. He offered his arm to Felicite.

“So you have abandoned your viscountess and her daughter for us,” she said, pressing his arm; “we are able now to understand the full merit of that sacrifice.”

“Are these Kergarouets related to the Portendueres?” asked Madame de Rochefide.

“The viscountess is the admiral’s great-niece,” replied Camille.

“Well, she’s a charming girl,” said Beatrix, placing herself in a Gothic chair. “She will just do for you, Monsieur du Guenic.”

“The marriage will never take place,” said Camille hastily.

Mortified by the marquise’s calm air toward the Breton girl, Calyste remained speechless.

“Why so, Camille?” asked Beatrix.

“Really, my dear,” said Camille, seeing Calyste’s despair, “you are not generous; did I advise Conti to marry?”

Beatrix looked with mingled surprise and suspicion. Calyste, sensing Camille’s support and emotion, took her hand; she left him and sat at the piano, improvising melancholy variations.

Beatrix watched Calyste more than listened. After an hour Beatrix retired to her apartments. Camille took Calyste into her chamber and closed the door, fearing to be overheard.

“My child,” she said, “if you want to succeed with Beatrix, you must seem to love me still, or you will fail. You are a child; you know nothing of women; all you know is how to love. Now loving and making one’s self beloved are two different things. If you go your own way you will fall into horrible suffering, and I wish to see you happy. If you rouse her pride or self-will, she is capable of going to Paris and rejoining Conti; and what will you do then?”

“I shall love her.”

“You won’t see her again.”

“Oh! yes, I shall,” he said.

“How?”

“I shall follow her.”

“Why, you are as poor as Job, my dear.”

“My father, Gasselin, and I lived for three months in Vendee on one hundred and fifty francs, marching night and day.”

“Calyste,” said Mademoiselle des Touches, “listen. I know you have too much candor to play a part, too much honesty to deceive; and I don’t want to corrupt you. Yet deception is the only way by which you can win Beatrix; I take it upon myself. In a week she shall love you.”

“Is it possible?” he cried clasping his hands.

“Yes,” replied Camille, “but you must obey me blindly. Come every day and devote yourself to me. Avoid Beatrix if you meet her. We will stay together till four o’clock; you shall study while I smoke. I will buy you books. The evenings we will spend together, and I permit you to make love to me if you can--it will be for the best.”

“I know, Camille, that your affection for me is great,” he replied; “but I don’t see what you hope from all this.”

“I hope to make her love you.”

“Good heavens! it cannot be possible!” he cried.

“Now listen carefully,” she said. “If you break our agreement, if you speak privately with the marquise, if you let her question you, if you fail in the silent part I ask, you will lose her forever.”

“I don’t understand,” cried Calyste.

“If you did, you wouldn’t be the noble and beautiful Calyste that you are,” she replied, taking his hand and kissing it.

Calyste then, for the first time, took Camille round the waist and kissed her gently with tenderness, not love. Mademoiselle des Touches did not restrain her tears.

“Go now,” she said, “my child; and tell your viscountess that my carriage is at her command.”

Calyste went home gaily, believing that in a week Beatrix would love him. The players at mouche found him once more the Calyste they had missed. Charlotte attributed his change to herself. The tournament of cards lasted till eleven. Mariotte had made galettes of buckwheat, and the du Guenics served a little supper before the departure of the guests. The modest hospitality had a charming effect.

A few moments after Calyste left Les Touches, Beatrix, who had heard him go, returned to Camille and found her with humid eyes.

“What is it, Felicite?” asked the marquise.

“I am forty years old, and I love him!” said Mademoiselle des Touches, with agony. “If you knew, Beatrix, the tears I have shed over lost youth! To be loved out of pity! to owe one’s happiness to perpetual care, to traps for innocence--oh, it is infamous! If it were not for the absolution in love’s magnitude, I would--if he asked it,--I would fling myself into the sea. Sometimes I wish he would ask it; it would be an oblation. Ah, Beatrix, by coming here you have set me a hard task. I know it will be difficult to keep him against you; but you love Conti, you are noble and generous, you will not deceive me; on the contrary, you will help me to retain Calyste’s love. I expected the impression you would make, but I have not seemed jealous; that would only have added fuel. I described you so glowing that you hardly realize the portrait, though you are more beautiful than ever.”

This mix of truth and deception duped the marquise. Claude Vignon had told Conti the reasons for his departure, and Beatrix was informed of them. She resolved to behave generously and give Calyste the cold shoulder; yet she felt the quiver of joy in being beloved. The homage a woman inspires in any man’s heart is flattery, and when the man belongs to a friend it gives delight. Beatrix sat beside her friend and began to coax her prettily.

“You have not a white hair,” she said; “you haven’t even a wrinkle.” She showed Camille the slight withering her own journey had cost her: a faint line at the wrists and temples.

“Your letter told me all,” replied Camille; “happiness ignores all but itself. You boasted too much of yours to be really happy. Truth is deaf, dumb, and blind where love is. Seeing that you have reasons to leave Conti, I feared to have you here. Calyste is an angel; his innocent heart will not resist your eyes. I am what I have never been--a child! I am sure of him, but I like to play at jealousy; he likes it too. Besides that is part of my secret.”

Beatrix, between jealousy and generosity, floated; she consented to bed piqued by curiosity and full of Calyste. Camille, when alone, burst into tears, smoked opium-soaked tobacco and saw the young head she had lost.

“What a glorious book to write, if I were only to express my pain!” she said. “Sappho lived before me. And Sappho was young. My poor Camille, smoke your hookah; you haven’t even the resource of making a poem of your misery!”

The next morning Calyste came before mid-day to Camille’s room and found books. Felicite sat by the window, smoking, contemplating the marshes, the sea, and Calyste, and now and then said a few words about Beatrix. She raised a curtain to attract the marquise’s attention and throw a band of light across Calyste’s book.

“Today stay to dinner; but you must refuse with a glance at the marquise that will show her how much you regret not staying,” she said.

When the three met in the salon and played this comedy, Calyste for a moment felt his equivocal position; the glance he cast at Beatrix was far more expressive than Felicite expected. Beatrix had dressed herself charmingly.

“What a bewitching toilet, my dearest!” said Camille when Calyste departed.

These manoeuvres lasted six days, during which Camille put all her ability into conversations with the marquise. They were like the preliminaries of a duel between two women, fought with snares, feints, false generosity, deceitful confessions, and crafty confidences by which one hid and the other bared her love. Camille’s treacherous words entered Beatrix’s heart and left poison. Beatrix took offence at what she thought Camille’s distrust; she was enchanted to find the great writer a victim to the pettiness of her sex and resolved to show where her greatness ended.

“My dear, what is to be the excuse today for Monsieur du Guenic’s not dining with us?” she asked mockingly. “Monday you had engagements; Tuesday the dinner was poor; Wednesday you feared his mother’s anger; Thursday a walk; yesterday you dismissed him. Today I shall have my way and mean that he shall stay.”

“Already, my dear!” said Camille with irony. The marquise blushed. “Stay, Monsieur du Guenic,” said Camille in the tone of a queen.

Beatrix became cold and hard, contradictory, and almost rude to Calyste, whom Felicite sent home to play mouche with Charlotte de Kergarouet.

“She is not dangerous at any rate,” said Beatrix, sarcastically.

Young lovers are like hungry men; kitchen odors do not appease their hunger. As Calyste walked back to Guerande, his soul was full of Beatrix; he paid no heed to Camille’s feminine cleverness. During the week the marquise had only written once to Conti, a symptom Camille imparted to Calyste. All his life was concentrated in their short meetings. The promise “Beatrix shall love you” was his talisman. He read through the night and brought back cartloads of books.

His aunt cursed Mademoiselle des Touches; his mother, seeing his late night study, suspected the secret. Though love was a sealed book to her, Fanny rose through maternal tenderness into some ideas of it; she was shocked at her son’s state. Calyste had one thought; Beatrix was always before him. His abstraction in the evenings resembled his father’s somnolence. Fanny implored him to give up Les Touches and leave the two women.

“Not go to Les Touches!” he cried.

“Oh! yes, yes, go! do not look so, my darling!” she cried, kissing him.

Under these circumstances Calyste nearly lost the fruit of Camille’s plot through the fury of his love. Finally he swore, in spite of his promise, to see Beatrix and speak to her. He wanted to read her eyes, study her dress, smell her perfume, hear her voice, and embrace, by a single glance, the whole figure.

One morning he resolved to go earlier than agreed and meet Beatrix in the garden. Mademoiselle des Touches and the marquise had just returned from the marshes and strolled up a garden path.

“If the scenery pleases you,” said Camille, “we must take Calyste to Croisic. There are splendid rocks, little bays, charmingly unexpected, besides the sea itself. Also you will see women making fuel with cow-dung.”

“What! will you really risk Calyste?” cried the marquise laughing.

“Ah, my dear,” replied Camille, “if you knew the angelic soul of that dear child, you would understand. In him, mere beauty is nothing; one must enter that pure heart, amazed at every step into the kingdom of love. What faith! what grace! what innocence! The ancients worshipped sacred beauty. Beauty is the genius of things; it is Nature’s ensign; Calyste is a vision realized. He has the regal power of a lion, tranquilly unsuspicious of its royalty. When at ease he is witty; I love his girlish timidity. My soul rests in his heart away from corruptions, knowledge, literature,--those accessories under which we stifle happiness. I am sure of him, but I like to play at jealousy; he likes it too. Besides, that is part of my secret.”

Beatrix walked pensively. Camille endured martydom, casting sidelong flame-like looks.

“Ah, my dear; but you are happy,” said Beatrix, laying her hand on Camille’s arm.

“Yes, happy indeed!” replied Felicite with savage bitterness.

They dropped upon a bench from exhaustion. No creature of her sex was ever played upon like an instrument with more Machiavellian penetration than Beatrix that week.

“Yes, you are happy, but I!” said Camille. “To know of Conti’s infidelities, and have to bear them!”

“Why not leave him?” said Camille, seeing the hour to strike decisive blow.

“Can I?” said Beatrix.

“Oh! poor boy!” they both gazed into a clump of trees stupefied.

Camille rose.

“I will go and hasten breakfast,” she said.

“Our conversation has taken away mine,” remarked Beatrix.

The marquise in her morning dress was outlined in white against the dark foliage. Calyste, who had slipped into the garden, took a path and met her. Beatrix could not restrain a quiver.

“Madame, in what way did I displease you yesterday?” he said after commonplace sentences.

“But you have neither pleased me nor displeased me,” she said gently.

The tone encouraged Calyste.

“Am I so indifferent to you?” he said as tears came to his eyes.

“Ought we not to be indifferent to each other? Have we not, each of us, another, and a binding attachment?”

“Oh!” cried Calyste, “if you mean Camille, I did love her, but I love her no longer.”

“Then why are you shut up together every morning?” she said with a treacherous smile. “I don’t suppose Camille prefers her cigar to you, or that you spend four hours a day reading her romances.”

“So then you know--” began Calyste, his face glowing.

“Calyste!” cried Camille angrily, appearing and interrupting him. She took his arm and drew him away. “Calyste, is this what you promised me?”

Beatrix heard the reproach and was stupefied by the young man’s assertion; she could not comprehend it. The part played by Camille, as shocking as it was grand, is one of those wicked grandeurs women practise when driven to extremity: an abnegation that breaks hearts and either plunges them to hell or lifts them to heaven.

During breakfast, invited to share, the marquise resolved to stifle the germs of love rising in her. She was neither cold nor hard to Calyste, but gently indifferent; this tortured him. Felicite proposed an excursion in two days to the country between Les Touches, Croisic, and Batz and asked Calyste to hire a boat and sailors. Beatrix stopped the plan, saying she did not wish to make excursions.

Calyste’s face, which had beamed, clouded.

“What are you afraid of, my dear?” asked Camille.

“My position is so delicate I do not wish to compromise my happiness,” said Beatrix, with a glance at Calyste. “You know how suspicious Conti can be; if he knew--”

“Who will tell him?”

“He is coming back here to fetch me,” said Beatrix.

Calyste turned pale. In spite of Camille’s urgings and his entreaties, Beatrix remained inflexible, showing the obstinacy Camille had warned about. Calyste left Les Touches the victim of one of those depressions of love that threaten to become madness. He began to revolve plans for an explanation with Beatrix.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
When Calyste reached home he did not leave his room until dinner; after dinner he returned to it. At ten o’clock his mother, uneasy at his absence, found him writing amid a pile of blotted and half-torn paper. He was composing a letter to Beatrix; distrust of Camille had entered his mind after the marquise’s manner in the garden had encouraged him.

No first love-letter is a brilliant literary effort; it pours from the heart and repeats itself in many revisions. This is the one Calyste finally wrote and read aloud to his astonished mother, who felt the old mansion burn with the blaze of her son’s love.

Calyste to Madame la Marquise de Rochefide.

Madame,—I loved you when you were but a dream; judge then of the force my love gained when I saw you. The dream was surpassed by the reality. I have nothing to say of your beauty and charm that you do not know, yet perhaps no other heart has felt such deep sentiment as mine.

I have studied you day and night, penetrated the mysteries of your being, the delicacy so little appreciated. Have you ever been loved, understood, adored as you deserve? Not a trait of yours escapes my heart; your pride is understood by mine; the grandeur of your glance, your grace, your distinction—everything harmonizes with the thoughts and desires I divined in you; hence I think myself worthy of your notice. If I had not, in the last few days, become another yourself, I could not speak of myself; this letter relates more to you than to me.

Beatrix, to write to you I have silenced my youth, laid aside myself, aged my thoughts—by this week of dreadful sufferings you have innocently caused. Do not think me one of those common lovers you laugh at. What merit is there in loving a beautiful, noble woman? I am a child attracted as insects to light. You cannot do otherwise than tread upon the flowers of my soul; all my happiness is in your stepping on them.

Absolute devotion, unbounded faith, love unquenchable—these treasures are nothing if they cannot win the love we crave. Sometimes I do not understand why such worship does not warm its idol; when I meet your cold, stern eye I turn to ice within. Your disdain, that governs between us, not my worship. You cannot hate me as much as I love you; why should the weaker feeling rule? I loved Felicite with all my heart, yet forgot her in a moment when I saw you. She was my error; you are my truth.

You have unknowingly destroyed my happiness, though you owe me nothing. I loved Camille without hope, and I have no hope from you; only my divinity has changed—I was a pagan, I am now a Christian. You have taught me that to love is the greatest joy; being loved comes later. Camille said love that does not grow is a wretched passion; she foresaw the end of ours. That noble Camille mingled her tears with mine yet rejected a love she saw must end. Therefore I am free to love you here on earth and in heaven. If you loved me you would have no such arguments as Camille used; we are young and could fly on equal wing across a sunny heaven.

But what am I saying? My thoughts exceed my hopes. Believe in the submission, patience, mute adoration; I only ask you not to wound uselessly. I know you cannot love me without losing self-esteem; therefore I ask no return. Camille once spoke of a fatality in names; I felt it on the jetty of Guerande when I read your name. You will pass through my life as Beatrice passed through Dante’s; my heart will be a pedestal for that white statue—cold, distant, jealous, oppressive.

It is forbidden to you to love me; you will suffer betrayals and humiliations, bound by a devil’s pride to the column you embraced; you are like Samson, perishing by clinging to it. But I have not divined this—my love is too blind; Camille told me. Blood gushes from my heart and darkens my intellect; I can only adore you whatever you may do.

Camille calls your resolution obstinacy; I call it virtue. You are more beautiful for it. I know my destiny; the pride of a Breton can rise to the height of a woman who makes pride a virtue.

Dear Beatrix, be kind and consoling. When victims were crowned with flowers, crown me with pity; you owe me the music of my sacrifice. Am I not proof of your grandeur? Will you not rise to my disdained love? Ask Camille how I behaved when she told me she loved Claude Vignon; I suffered in silence. For you I will show greater strength—I will bury my feelings if you do not drive me to despair. A single word of praise from you will make me bear martyrdom.

But if you persist in cold silence you will make me think you fear me. Be with me what you are—charming, witty, gay, tender. Talk to me of Conti as Camille has talked to me of Claude. I have no other genius than that of love; nothing in me should make you fear. I will be in your presence as if I loved you not.

Can you refuse so humble a prayer? The child only asks that his Light shall lighten him, his Sun warm him.

The man you love can be with you at all times, but I have so few days in which to see you; you will soon be freed from me. May I return to Les Touches tomorrow? Will you not refuse my arm for that excursion? We shall go to Croisic and Batz; if you do not go I shall take it for an answer—Calyste will understand.

There were four more pages of similar, close, impassioned writing where Calyste expounded his youth and life, mostly exclamatory and repetitive. Though unlikely to move Madame de Rochefide, it made his mother weep.

“My child, you are not happy,” she said in her tender voice. The tumultuous poem rising like a storm terrified the baroness; for the first time she read a love-letter. Calyste, perplexed about how to send it, followed his mother into the salon with the letter burning in his pocket. The Chevalier du Halga was there; Charlotte de Kergarouet tried to use Calyste’s indifference to further her own aims. Calyste wandered like a distracted butterfly. When the card game ended he drew the chevalier aside.

“What is the best way to send a letter secretly to one’s mistress?” he asked in a whisper.

Slip it into the maid’s hand with a louis or two, the chevalier suggested—though on the whole it was best to give the letter oneself. Calyste snatched his hat and ran to Les Touches, where he appeared like an apparition in the little salon, where Camille and Beatrix sat together on the sofa. Calyste flung himself impulsively beside the marquise, took her hand, and slipped the letter into it so rapidly that Felicite did not perceive it. His heart tingled as Beatrix pressed his hand and, without interrupting her words, slipped the letter into her glove.

“You fling yourself on a woman’s dress without mercy,” she laughed.

“Calyste is wanting in common sense,” said Felicite, openly rebuking him.

Calyste kissed Camille’s hand, then ran his fingernail over the piano keys in a rapid scale. His exuberance surprised Camille, who signed for him to come near.

“What is the matter with you?” she whispered.

“Nothing,” he said.

“There is something between them,” thought Mademoiselle des Touches. The marquise was impenetrable. Camille tried to draw speech from Calyste, hoping his artless mind would betray itself; he excused himself because his mother expected him, and left Les Touches at eleven, after facing a piercing glance from Camille.

That wakeful night Calyste searched the main street of Guerande many times for an answer. At last the marquise’s waiting-woman brought him a note, which he read in the garden grotto.

Madame de Rochefide to Calyste.

You are a noble child, but only a child. You are bound to Camille, who adores you. You would not find in me the perfections that distinguish her or the happiness she can give. Whatever you may think, she is young and I am old; her heart is full of treasures, mine empty; she is unselfish and lives for you, while I am full of doubts and a slave.

You forget I love and am beloved. My situation forbids accepting homage; a man saying he loves me is an insult. To yield would lower me to the level of the lowest of my sex. You who are young and pure are carried away by fancy; you cannot make the reflections evil suggests. I will go further: were I humiliated, betrayed, abandoned—no one in the world would see me more. I believe I should find courage to kill a man who, seeing me thus, spoke to me of love.

You now know my mind. After receiving your letter and making you this reply I could be at my ease in Camille’s house and be what you ask; but the bitter ridicule that would ensue were I to soften my manner is dreadful. A second robbery from Camille would prove her want of power which no woman could forgive. Even if I loved you, Camille’s love for you is a barrier too high to be overleaped; none but a devil would fail to recoil before such treachery. There are motives delicate women keep to themselves which you men cannot understand.

Your mother is pure and spotless; she fulfils her destiny nobly. She is the woman your wife should be. I will never send you back, in jest, to little Charlotte; I commend you to some divine young girl worthy of your love.

If I were yours your life would be blighted. I should have taken your existence and made you unhappy; I am jealous—lions lurk in the path, monsters in drops of water. Trifles would make me wretched; inexorable thoughts from my heart would poison our existence. If after ten years a man were not as respectful and delicate, I should resent the change; such a lover could not believe in the Amadis and Cyrus of my dreams. True love to-day is but a dream; in yours I see merely the joy of desire whose end you do not yet perceive.

I am not forty; I have not bent my pride beneath experience; I may be too young to be anything but odious. I will not answer for my temper; my grace and charm are external. Perhaps I have not suffered enough to have the indulgent manners and tenderness born of cruel disappointments. Camille will always be your devoted slave; I should be an unreasonable tyrant.

Felicite’s tenderness is inexhaustible; she will marry you to some young girl, no matter what she suffers; she will smooth difficulties for you. The sale of an acre in Paris would free your estate in Brittany; she will make you her heir—you are already her son by adoption.

Alas! what could I do for your happiness? Nothing. Do not betray that infinite love which contents itself with the duties of motherhood. Camille is fortunate; she can forgive your fancy. I have studied her closely: she has mind and goodness, is generous and simple—rare amalgamations. Dante’s line about eternal happiness, “Senza brama sicura ricchezza,” seems made for her. She has told me of her career and how love has eluded her; she seems an example of the difficulty, perhaps impossibility, of uniting two great glories.

You, Calyste, are an angelic soul whose mate seems impossible to find; but Camille will obtain for you the hand of a young girl with whom you can make a happy home, even if she dies in doing so.

For myself, I hold out a friendly hand; I count on your mind to make you a brother, as I shall be a sister; let this letter end a correspondence between Les Touches and Guerande that is rather absurd.

Beatrix de Casteran.

The baroness, stirred by her son’s strange exhibitions and rapid changes, could not sit quietly. After looking at him from time to time, she rose and came to him humbly yet boldly to demand a favor she felt entitled to ask. Calyste read Beatrix’s letter aloud. These two noble, guileless souls saw no malice in the marquise’s cautious epistle.

“She is a noble, grand woman!” said the baroness with moist eyes. “I will pray for her. I did not know a woman could abandon her husband and child and yet keep a soul so virtuous. She is worthy of pardon.”

“Have I not every reason to adore her?” cried Calyste.

“But where will this love lead you?” said the baroness. “How dangerous are women with noble sentiments! There is less to fear from the bad. Marry Charlotte de Kergarouet and release two-thirds of the estate. By selling a few farms, Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel can secure that result in the marriage contract and help you manage your property. You could leave your children a great name and estate.”

“Forget Beatrix!” said Calyste, muffled, eyes on the ground.

He left and went to his room to write an answer.

Madame du Guenic, whose heart had kept every word of Beatrix’s letter, sought help to understand it and Calyste’s hope. The Chevalier du Halga, who frequented the mall with his dog, was found and the baroness, certain to meet him, went out. The sight of her walking in Guerande was an event; townspeople talked of it. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel told her niece Charlotte, “Something very extraordinary is happening at the du Guenics.”

“Calyste is madly in love with that beautiful Marquise de Rochefide,” Charlotte said. “I ought to leave Guerande.”

The chevalier, surprised to be sought, unhooked his dog. “Chevalier,” began the baroness, “you used to practise gallantry?” He straightened with vain air as she, without naming Calyste or the marquise, recited the love-letter and asked his opinion of Beatrix’s reply. Du Halga listened, made grimaces, and finally—“When thoroughbred horses want to leap a barrier, they go up to reconnoitre it. Calyste is a lucky dog!” The baroness cried “Oh, hush!” He rambled into old exploits, but escorted her to the alley, faithful to gallant forms, neglecting Thisbe.

The baroness ran to Calyste’s room. He was absent; she saw an unsealed letter addressed to Madame de Rochefide on the table and, compelled by invincible curiosity, read it. The indiscretion punished her: the letter revealed how deep the gulf into which Calyste’s passion was hurling him plunged.

Calyste to Madame la Marquise de Rochefide.

What care I for the race of the du Guenics in these days, Beatrix? Their name is nothing to me. My name is Beatrix; your happiness is my happiness; your life is my life; all my fortune is in your heart. Our estates have been mortgaged for two hundred years; so they may remain; our farmers have them; no one can take them from us. To see you, to love you—that is my property, my object, my religion!

You talk to me of marrying! The thought convulses me. Is there another Beatrix? I will marry no one but you; I will wait twenty years if need be. I am young, and you will be ever beautiful. My mother is a saint; she has never loved; you have taught me what she lost, and what sacrifices she made. You have taught me to love my mother better; she is in my heart with you—no other can be there; she is your only rival—you reign supreme.

As for Camille, you need only say the word and I will ask her to tell you she is not loved by me. She is the mother of my intellect, nothing more. From the moment I saw you she became a sister, a friend, a comrade; we have rights only of friendship. I took her for a woman until I saw you. You have proved that Camille is a man—she swims, hunts, smokes, drinks, rides, writes and analyzes hearts—she has no woman’s weaknesses; her movements lack your grace. In you are all the things of womanhood I love. From that first moment I felt you were mine.

You will laugh, but the fancy has grown. It seems unnatural we should be apart. You are my soul; I cannot live where you are not!

Let me love you! Let us fly to a country where you know no one, where only God and I can reach your heart! My mother might follow. Ireland is full of castles; my mother’s family will lend us one. Beatrix, let us go! A boat, a few sailors, and we are there before any one knows we have fled.

You have never been loved. I see in your letter that if such reasons did not exist you might let yourself be loved by me. A sacred love wipes out the past. I love you so truly I could wish you doubly shamed if so my love might prove itself by making you a saint!

You call my love an insult. Oh, Beatrix, you do not mean it! The love of noble youth—one you called noble—would honor a queen. Tomorrow let us walk as lovers by the sea; your step on my sands will bless them anew. Give me this day of happiness; that passing alms from you will be eternal riches to your

Calyste.

The baroness let the letter fall before reading all. She knelt on a chair and prayed to God to save her son’s reason, to put his madness and error far from him. Calyste entered.

“What are you doing, mother?” he asked.

“I am praying to God for you,” she answered simply, turning tearful eyes on him. “I have committed the sin of reading that letter. My Calyste is mad!”

“A sweet madness!” he said, kissing her.

“I wish I could see that woman,” she sighed.

“Mamma,” Calyste answered, “we shall take a boat to-morrow and cross to Croisic. If you are on the jetty you can see her.”

So saying, he sealed his letter and departed for Les Touches. What especially terrified the baroness was that Calyste’s second letter showed the instinctive feeling and clear-sightedness of practiced experience; it read like something the Chevalier du Halga, with worldly knowledge, might have dictated.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Perhaps one of the greatest enjoyments for small or inferior minds is deceiving a great soul and setting snares for it. Beatrix knew herself far beneath Camille Maupin—this inferiority was not only in talent but in matters of the heart: passion.

When Calyste hurried to Les Touches with the impetuosity of first love, the marquise took keen delight in being the object of that affection. She did not wish to share the sentiment; she fancied herself heroic in repressing her caprice. She imagined she matched Camille’s devotion and persuaded herself she sacrificed for her friend. Her vanities, that notable coquetry, were flattered by Calyste’s love. Assailed by such seduction, she resisted it, praising her virtues inwardly.

The two women half-sat, half-reclined on the little salon divan, the room a scene of harmony and fragrance. The windows stood open; the north wind had ceased, a southerly breeze ruffled the salt lake, and the sun glittered on the shore sands. Their souls were as agitated as the scene was tranquil; the inward heat was ardent.

Bruised by the machinery she had set in motion, Camille guarded herself against the clever friendly enemy she had admitted. To preserve secrets and remain aloof, she contemplated nature; she cheated her aching heart by seeking meaning in the world, finding God in that desert of heaven and earth. When an unbeliever perceives God, he flings himself unreservedly into Catholicism, seen as a complete system.

That morning Camille wore the halo of thoughts born of these researches after a night of painful struggle. Calyste stood before her like a celestial image. The beautiful youth, to whom she had secretly devoted herself, had become a guardian angel. Did he not lift her into loftier regions where suffering ceased beneath the weight of infinity? Now a certain air of triumph about Beatrix disturbed her. No woman gains advantage without making it felt; the dumb moral struggle between these two, each hiding a secret and believing herself generous through hidden sacrifices, was strange.

Calyste arrived, the letter between his hand and glove ready to slip to Beatrix. Camille, noting a subtle change in her friend’s manner, watched Beatrix in a mirror at the moment Calyste entered. That sudden moment is crucial for women: the cleverest as well as the silliest, the frankest as the shrewdest, seldom keep secrets; they betray them in a look. Too much reserve or too little; a free luminous gaze; the mysterious lowering of eyelids—all betray what is hardest to hide; real indifference is too cold to simulate. Women have a genius for nuance; they can read a rival from head to foot. Two women observing each other play one of the world's choicest comedies.

“Calyste has committed some folly,” thought Camille, perceiving in each guest an indefinable air of mutual understanding.

Beatrix no longer feigned indifference; she regarded Calyste as her own. Calyste was even more transparent; he colored like guilty or happy people. He announced he had come to arrange the excursion for the following day.

“Then you really intend to go, my dear?” Camille asked.

“Yes,” said Beatrix.

“How did you know it, Calyste?” asked Mademoiselle des Touches.

“I came here to find out,” replied Calyste, prompted by a look from Madame de Rochefide, who did not want Camille to learn of their correspondence.

“They have an agreement together,” thought Camille, catching that look in its sweep.

Under that thought a horrible discomposure spread over her face and frightened Beatrix.

“What is the matter, my dear?” she cried.

“Nothing. Well, then, Calyste, send my horses and yours across to Croisic, so we may drive home by way of Batz. We will breakfast at Croisic and be home in time for dinner. You must arrange the boat. Let us start by half-past eight. You will see some fine sights, Beatrix, and one very strange one; you will see Cambremer, a man who does penance on a rock for having wilfully killed his son. Oh! you are in a primitive land, among a primitive race of people, moved by other sentiments than ordinary mortals. Calyste shall tell you the tale; it is a drama of the seashore.”

She went into her bedroom, stifling. Calyste gave his letter to Beatrix and followed Camille.

“Calyste, you are loved, I think; but you are hiding something from me; you have done some foolish thing.”

“Loved!” he exclaimed, sinking into a chair.

Camille glanced into the next room; Beatrix had disappeared—odd, since women seldom leave a room with the man they admire unless sure of seeing him again or for some advantage. Mademoiselle des Touches wondered, “Can he have given her a letter?” but thought the innocent Breton incapable of such boldness.

“If you have disobeyed me, all will be lost through your own fault,” she said gravely. “Go now and make your preparations for tomorrow.”

She made a gesture Calyste did not resist.

As he walked toward Croisic to hire boatmen, fears entered Calyste’s mind. Camille’s speech foreshadowed something fatal; he believed this maternal second sight. When he returned four hours later, tired and expecting to dine at Les Touches, he found Camille’s maid at the door to tell him that neither her mistress nor the marquise could receive him that evening. Calyste, surprised, wished to question her, but she bade him hastily good-night and closed the door.

Six o’clock was striking in Guerande as Calyste entered his house; Mariotte gave him his belated dinner, after which he played mouche in gloomy meditation. Alternations of joy and gloom, the extinction of hopes following seeming certainties of being loved, bruised the young soul that had flown so high that the fall was dreadful.

“Does anything trouble you, my Calyste?” his mother asked.

“Nothing,” he replied, his eyes lacking their usual light.

It is not hope but despair that measures our ambitions. The finest poems of hope are sung in secret; grief appears unveiled.

“Calyste, you are not nice,” said Charlotte, after vain attempts at the provincial witcheries that turn to teasing.

“I am tired,” he said, rising and bidding the company good-night.

“Calyste is much changed,” remarked Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel.

“We haven’t beautiful dresses trimmed with lace; we don’t shake our sleeves like this, or twist our bodies like that; we don’t know how to give sidelong glances and turn our eyes,” said Charlotte, mimicking the marquise’s air and attitudes. “We haven’t that head voice, nor the interesting little cough heu! heu! like a spook’s sigh; we are healthy and robust, and love our friends without coquetry; and when we look at them, we don’t pretend to stick a dart into them or watch them slyly; we can’t bend our heads like a weeping willow just to look more interesting—this way.”

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel laughed, but neither the chevalier nor the baron paid heed to this provincial satire against Paris.

“But the Marquise de Rochefide is a very handsome woman,” the old maid said.

“My dear,” said the baroness to her husband, “I know she is going to Croisic tomorrow. Let us walk on the jetty; I should like to see her.”

While Calyste tried to guess what had closed Les Touches’ doors to him, a scene between Camille and Beatrix unfolded that would influence the morrow.

Calyste’s last letter stirred in Madame de Rochefide’s heart emotions hitherto unknown to it. Women seldom are the object of love so young, guileless, sincere, and unconditional as his. Beatrix, who had loved more than she had been loved, suddenly desired to be a tyrant. Yet reading and rereading the letter, a cruel idea pierced her.

What were Calyste and Camille doing together since Claude Vignon’s departure? If Calyste did not love Camille, and Camille knew it, how did they spend their mornings alone? Memory flashed certain speeches of Camille into Beatrix’s mind; a grinning devil seemed to show her, like a magic mirror, that heroic woman’s portrait with gestures and aspects that enlightened her. What! Instead of being Camille’s equal, was she crushed by Félicité? Instead of outwitting her, had she been outwitted? Was she merely a toy, a pleasure Camille gave to her child, whom she loved with an extraordinary, nonvulgar passion?

To Beatrix such a thought was a thunderclap. She reviewed the past week minutely; the roles each had played unrolled before her; she felt horribly belittled. In her fury of jealous anger she fancied Camille’s conduct an intention of vengeance against Conti. Was hidden wrath from the past two years affecting the present?

Once on that path of doubts, Beatrix did not pause. She paced, sat, tried to decide, unable to; she remained prey to indecision until dinner, when she rose hastily and went downstairs without dressing. As Camille saw her in a morning gown, with chilling air and taciturn manner, she felt a crisis had come. Beatrix’s look indicated the coming hostilities of an embittered heart.

Camille instantly left the room and gave the order surprising Calyste; she feared he might arrive amid the quarrel and wished to fight alone, without witnesses. Beatrix alone would infallibly succumb. Camille knew the other’s soul was barren and pride petty and obstinate.

The dinner was gloomy. Camille was gentle and kind; she felt superior. Beatrix was hard and cutting; she felt managed like a child. The battle began with glances, gestures, half-spoken sentences—insufficient to enlighten servants but enough to prepare an observer for the storm. When they rose, Camille offered her arm maliciously to Beatrix, who pretended not to see and sprang up the stair alone. After coffee, Mademoiselle des Touches dismissed the footman with “You may go”—a brief sentence serving as their signal.

“The novels you make, my dear, are more dangerous than those you write,” said the marquise.

“They have one advantage, however,” Camille replied, lighting a cigarette.

“What is that?” asked Beatrix.

“They are unpublished, my angel.”

“Is the one in which you are putting me to be turned into a book?”
