“I’ve no fancy for the role of Oedipus; I know you have the wit and beauty of a sphinx, but don’t propound conundrums. Speak out plainly, my dear Beatrix.”

“When, to make a man happy, amuse him, please him, and save him from ennui, we let the devil help us—”

“That man would reproach us later for our efforts and would think them prompted by the genius of depravity,” interrupted Camille, taking the cigarette from her lips.

“He forgets the love that carried us away; that’s our sole justification—but men are unjust and ungrateful,” Beatrix continued. “Women know each other; they know how proud and noble their minds are, and—frankly—how virtuous! But, Camille, I’ve just recognized some criticisms of your nature you’ve sometimes complained of. You have something of the man about you; you behave like a man; nothing restrains you; if you haven’t all a man’s advantages, you have his spirit and his contempt for women. I have no reason to be satisfied with you and am too frank to hide my dissatisfaction. No one has ever given or will give so cruel a wound as the one I now suffer. If you are not a woman in love, you are one in vengeance. It takes a woman of genius to find the most sensitive spot in another woman’s delicacy. I speak now of Calyste and the trickery—you have employed against me. To what depths have you descended, Camille Maupin! and why?”

“More and more sphinx-like!” said Camille, smiling.

“You want me to fling myself at Calyste’s head; but I am still too young for that. To me, love is sacred; love is love with all its emotions, jealousies, and despotisms. I am not an author; I cannot see ideas where the heart feels sentiments.”

“You think yourself capable of loving foolishly!” said Camille. “Make yourself easy; you have plenty of sense. You calumniate yourself; your nature is cold enough to let your head judge your heart.”

The marquise colored; she darted a venomous look and found the sharpest arrows in her quiver. Camille smoked composedly as Beatrix launched a furious tirade and even attacked Camille’s age.

“Is that all?” said Félicité, when Beatrix paused, letting smoke exhale.

“Do you love Calyste?”

“No; of course not.”

“So much the better,” Camille replied. “I do love him—far too much for my peace of mind. He may perhaps have had a passing fancy for you; for you are enchantingly fair, while I am as black as a crow; you are slim and willowy, while I have portly dignity; in short, you are young!—that’s the final word, and you have not spared it to me. You have abused your advantages as a woman against me. I have done my best to prevent what has now happened. However little woman you may think me, I am woman enough not to allow a rival to triumph unless I choose to help her.” (This remark, apparently innocent, cut the marquise to the heart.) “You take me for a very silly person if you believe all that Calyste tries to make you think of me. I am neither so great nor so small; I am a woman, and very much of a woman. Come, put off your grand airs, and give me your hand!” Camille took Madame de Rochefide’s hand. “You do not love Calyste, you say; that is true, is it not? Don’t be angry; be hard, cold, and stern to him tomorrow; he will end by submitting, especially after certain reproaches I mean to make. Still, Calyste is a Breton, and very persistent; if he continues to court you, tell me frankly, and I will lend you my little country house near Paris, where you will find comforts, and where Conti can come out and see you. You said Calyste calumniated me. Good heavens! what of that? The purest love lies twenty times a day; its deceptions prove its strength.”

Camille’s superb disdain frightened Beatrix, who did not know how to answer. Camille dealt a last blow.

“I am more confiding and less bitter than you,” she said. “I don’t suspect you of attempting to cover by a quarrel a secret injury that would compromise my life. You know me; I shall never survive the loss of Calyste, but I must lose him sooner or later. Still, Calyste loves me now; of that I am sure.”

“Here is what he answered to my letter urging him to be true to you,” said Beatrix, holding out Calyste’s last letter.

Camille read it; her eyes filled with tears; presently she wept as women weep in their bitterest sorrows.

“My God!” she said, “how he loves her! I shall die without being understood—or loved,” she added.

She sat with her head on Beatrix’s shoulder; her grief was genuine, the same terrible blow the Baronne du Guenic had felt reading that letter.

“Do you love him?” she asked, looking fixedly at Beatrix. “Have you that infinite worship for him which triumphs over all pains, survives contempt, betrayal, the certainty that he will never love you? Do you love him for himself, and for the joy of loving him?”

“Dear friend,” said the marquise tenderly, “be happy, be at peace; I will leave this place tomorrow.”

“No, do not go; he loves you, I see that. Well, I love him so much I could not endure to see him wretched. I had formed plans, projects; but if he loves you, all is over.”

“And I love him, Camille,” said the marquise, with some naïveté and coloring.

“You love him, and yet you cast him off!” cried Camille. “Ah! that is not loving; you do not love him.”

“I don’t know what fresh virtue he has roused in me, but certainly he has made me ashamed of myself,” said Beatrix. “I would I were virtuous and free, to give him something better than the dregs of a heart and the weight of my chains. I do not want a hampered destiny for him or myself.”

“Cold brain!” exclaimed Camille, with horror. “To love and calculate!”

“Call it what you like,” Beatrix replied, “but I will not spoil his life, or hang like a millstone round his neck to become an eternal regret. If I cannot be his wife, I shall not be his mistress. He has— you will laugh? No? Well, then, he has purified me.”

Camille cast the most sullen, savage look female jealousy can cast.

“On that ground, I believed I stood alone,” she said. “Beatrix, those words must separate us forever; we are no longer friends. Here begins a terrible conflict between us. I tell you now; you will either succumb or fly.”

So saying, Camille bounded into her room, showing the maddened-lioness’s face to astonished Beatrix, then raised the portière and looked back in.

“Do you intend to go to Croisic tomorrow?” she asked.

“Certainly,” replied Beatrix proudly. “I shall not fly, and I shall not succumb.”

“I play above board,” replied Camille; “I shall write to Conti.”

Beatrix turned as white as her gauze scarf.

“We are staking our lives on this game,” she murmured, not knowing what to say or do.

The violent passions roused by this scene calmed during the night. Both argued with themselves and returned to the temporizing courses attractive to most women—an excellent system between men and women, but fatally unsafe among women alone. In the midst of this tumult Mademoiselle des Touches listened to that great Voice whose counsels subdue the strongest will; Beatrix heard only the promptings of worldly wisdom; she feared society’s contempt.

Thus Félicité’s last deception succeeded; Calyste’s blunder was repaired, but a fresh indiscretion might yet be fatal to him.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
It was the end of August, and the sky was magnificently clear. Near the horizon the sea had the molten-silver tint of southern waters; on the shore it rippled in tiny waves. A glowing vapor from the sun on the sands produced an almost tropical atmosphere. Salt gleamed like bunches of white violets on the marsh surface. The patient paludiers, dressed in white against the sun and armed with long rakes, had been at their posts since morning. Some leaned on the low mud-walls dividing the holdings, watching the salt-making known to them from childhood; others played with their wives and children. The custom-house officers smoked their pipes with calm.

The scene had an exotic air; a Parisian suddenly transported here would hardly guess he was in France. The baron and baroness, who had feigned interest in the salt harvest, stood on the jetty admiring the silent landscape where only the sea sounded, and where boats and the cultivated green girdle of earth made a rare charm on the ocean shore.

“Well, my friends, I wanted to see the marshes of Guerande once more before I die,” said the baron to the paludiers gathered at the marsh entrance.

“Can a Guenic die?” replied one.

The party from Les Touches arrived by the narrow path. The marquise walked first alone; Calyste and Camille followed arm-in-arm, Gasselin bringing up the rear.

“There are my father and mother,” Calyste said to Camille.

The marquise stopped. Madame du Guenic felt the strongest repulsion for Beatrix, though Beatrix was well dressed: a wide Leghorn hat with bluebells, crimped hair, a gray wool gown and a blue sash gave her the air of a princess disguised as a milkmaid.

“She has no heart,” thought the baroness.

“Mademoiselle,” Calyste said to Camille, “this is Madame du Guenic, and this is my father.” Then to the baron and baroness: “Mademoiselle des Touches, and Madame la Marquise de Rochefide, née de Casteran, father.”

The baron bowed; Mademoiselle des Touches made a respectful bow full of gratitude to the baroness.

“That one,” Fanny thought, “really loves my boy; she seems to thank me for bringing him into the world.”

“I suppose you have come to see, as I have, whether the harvest is a good one. But I believe you have better reasons,” said the baron to Camille. “You have property here, I think.”

“Mademoiselle is the largest owner,” said one of the paludiers, “and may God preserve her to us, for she’s a good lady.”

The parties bowed and separated.

“No one would suppose Mademoiselle des Touches to be more than thirty,” said the baron. “She is very handsome. And Calyste prefers that haggard Parisian marquise to a sound Breton girl!”

“I fear he does,” replied the baroness.

A boat awaited at the jetty steps; the party embarked in silence. The marquise was cold and dignified. Camille had lectured Calyste on his disobedience, making the situation clear. Calyste, in black despair, cast glances at Beatrix where anger and love struggled. Not a word was spoken during the short passage from the jetty of Guerande to the port of Croisic, where salt is discharged and peasant-women carry it away in earthen jars on their heads. These women, barefoot with short petticoats, sometimes let their kerchiefs fly open; some wearing only shifts appeared more dignified, for their modest bearing seemed nobler with less clothing.

The little Danish vessel had just finished lading; the landing of two handsome ladies excited curiosity among the salt-carriers. To avoid their remarks and to serve Calyste, Camille sprang toward the rocks, leaving him to follow with Beatrix. Gasselin put distance between himself and his master.

The Croisic peninsula is flanked by granite rocks whose fantastic shapes defy easy comparison. The rocks form interminable arabesques and the imagination tires amid this gallery of abnormal shapes, where the sea in storm polishes the forms. Under a natural vaulted roof, like a bold effect art imitates, is a rocky hollow polished like a marble bathtub floored with white sand and tepid water safe for bathing. Cool little covers sheltered by great portals, roughly carved but majestic, recall palaces that imitate nature’s whims. Curious features are innumerable.

There is one rare thing here: a large box-bush, perhaps unique on such rocky shores, three miles from the harbor on the point of rocks that runs far into the sea. On a granite promontory, high and out of the spray, chance or birds have placed enough vegetable earth for this compact plant, whose roots indicate an existence of perhaps three hundred years. Above it the rock breaks off abruptly; the convulsion that did this left traces marked here.

The sea rushes to the foot of this cliff, which rises four or five hundred feet; scattered rocks at its base describe a semi-circle. It takes nerve to climb this little Gibraltar whose summit resembles old castle look-out towers. From it you see the clock towers and arid fields of Croisic, the sandy dunes that injure cultivation stretching to Batz. Sardine fishers gave the rock a Breton name best not written here.

Calyste led Beatrix to this point, where the view is magnificent and where the granite’s sculpture is even more imposing close at hand.

Camille, like a wounded wild animal longing for solitude, threaded the fissures and caves of nature’s fortress. Not to be hampered in climbing, she wore trousers with frilled edges, a short blouse, a peaked cap, and carried a riding-whip. In this attire she looked handsomer than Beatrix; she wore a little crimson China crape shawl crossed on her bosom and tied behind like a child. For some time Beatrix and Calyste saw her flitting over peaks and chasms like a ghost; she tried to still inward sufferings by confronting imaginary peril.

Camille was first to reach the rock where the box-bush grew. She sat in the shade, lost in thought. What could a woman like her do with old age, having already drunk the cup of fame too eagerly? There, on that spot, a singular reflection—one of those chance accidents that open vast depths of thought to noble souls—decided her to take an extraordinary step to part forever from social life.

She drew from her pocket a little box of strawberry lozenges, ate several, and thought: the strawberries no longer exist, yet live in their qualities. Was it not so with ourselves? The ocean was an image of the infinite. No great spirit can face the infinite and admit immortality without the conviction of a future of holiness. The thought filled her. How petty then seemed the part she had been playing! There was no true greatness in giving Beatrix to Calyste. Thinking so, the earthly woman died within her; the noble and angelic being veiled by flesh arose. Her mind, knowledge, attainments, and false loves confronted the bounteous mother and comforter of troubled spirits—the Roman Church—poetic to poets, childlike with children, and profound to anxious souls. She saw her love for Calyste as a divine messenger who had turned earthly desire to divine love.

After walking in silence, Calyste could not refrain from comparing the ocean’s depth and purity with his love when Beatrix remarked on the ocean’s grandeur, unlike the Mediterranean.

“It is met by a rock!” said Beatrix, laughing.

“When you speak thus,” he answered with a sublime look, “I hear you, I see you, and I can summon the patience of the angels; but when I am alone, you would pity me if you could see me then. My mother weeps for my suffering.”

“Listen to me, Calyste; we must put an end to all this,” said the marquise, gazing down upon the sandy plain. “Perhaps this is the only propitious place to say these things, for never in my life did I see nature more in keeping with my thoughts. I have seen Italy, where all tells of love; Switzerland, where all is cool and fresh and tells of labor’s happiness; but I have never seen anything that so depicts the burning barrenness of my life as that arid plain dried by salt winds, corroded by spray, where fruitless agriculture struggles against the ocean. There, Calyste, you have an image of this Beatrix. Don’t cling to it. I love you, but I will never be yours in any way, for I have the sense of my inward desolation. No, you shall never see your idol diminished; she shall never fall from the height at which you have placed her. I now have a horror of love which disregards the world and religion. I shall remain in my present bonds; I shall be that sandy plain—without fruit or flowers.”

“But if you are abandoned?” said Calyste.

“Then I should beg my pardon of the man I have offended. I will never risk taking a happiness I know would quickly end.”

“End!” cried Calyste.

The marquise repeated the word in a tone that silenced him. This opposition roused in Calyste a mute inward fury known only to those who love without hope. They walked for several hundred steps in silence, looking neither at sea nor rocks nor plain.

“I would make you happy,” said Calyste.

“All men begin by promising that,” she answered. “They end by abandonment and disgust. I have no reproach to cast on him to whom I shall be faithful. He made me no promises; I went to him; but my only means of lessening my fault is to make it eternal.”

“Say rather,” he cried, “that you feel no love for me. I, who love you, know love cannot argue; it is itself; it sees nothing else. There is no sacrifice I will not make to you; command it, and I will do the impossible. He who despised his mistress for flinging her glove among the lions and ordered him to bring it back did not love—he denied the right to test our hearts and to yield only to our utmost devotion. I will sacrifice my family, my name, my future.”

“What an insult in that word ‘sacrifice’!” she said reproachfully, which made Calyste feel the folly of his speech.

“You are right,” he replied, letting fall a tear; “that word can only be said of the cruel struggles you ask of me.”

“Hush!” said Beatrix, struck by an answer in which, for the first time, Calyste had really made her feel his love. “I have done wrong enough; tempt me no more.”

They reached the base of the rock where the box grew. Calyste helped her ascend the steep slope; to the poor lad it was a precious privilege to support her. This turned his head; he saw nothing but Beatrix and clasped her round the waist.

“What!” she said with an imposing air.

“Will you never be mine?” he demanded, voice choked with tumult.

“Never, my friend,” she replied. “I can only be to you a Beatrix—a dream. But is that not sweet and tender? We shall have no bitterness, no grief, no repentance.”

“Will you return to Conti?”

“I must.”

“You shall never belong to any man!” cried Calyste, pushing her with frenzied violence.

He listened for her fall, intending to spring after her, but heard only a muffled sound: the tearing of some stuff and then the thud of a body. Instead of being flung headlong down the precipice, Beatrix had slipped eight or ten feet into the cavity where the box-bush grew; her gown caught on a rock point and, tearing slowly, lowered her onto the bush.

Mademoiselle des Touches, horrified, could not cry out but signed to Gasselin to come. Calyste leaned forward, his expression savage curiosity; he saw Beatrix’s position and shuddered. Her lips moved—she seemed to be praying; she thought she was about to die as the bush began to give. With youth’s agility Calyste slid down to the ledge below the bush, grasped the marquise and held her though both risked sliding into the sea. She had fainted; in that aerial spot he fancied her wholly his, and his first emotion was pleasure.

“Open your eyes,” he said, “and forgive me; we will die together.”

“Die?” she said, opening her eyes and unclosing pallid lips.

Calyste welcomed the word with a kiss, and felt her tremble convulsively with passionate joy. At that instant Gasselin’s hob-nailed shoes sounded above them. The old Breton was followed by Camille; together they sought a way to save the lovers.

“There is but one way, mademoiselle,” said Gasselin. “I must slide down there; they can climb on my shoulders; you must pull them up.”

“And you?” said Camille.

The man seemed surprised to be considered. “You must go to Croisic and fetch a ladder,” she answered.

Beatrix asked to be laid down; Calyste placed her on the narrow space between the bush and rock. “I saw you, Calyste,” Camille said from above. “Whether Beatrix lives or dies, remember that this must be an accident.”

“She will hate me,” he said, moist-eyed.

“She will adore you,” replied Camille. “But this puts an end to our excursion. We must get her back to Les Touches. Had she been killed, Calyste, what would have become of you?”

“I should have followed her.”

“And your mother? And me?” After a pause she added feebly.

Calyste stood pale and silent. Gasselin returned from a nearby farm with a ladder. By then Beatrix had recovered somewhat. With the ladder placed and Gasselin lowering Camille’s red shawl for her to grasp, Beatrix reached the top where the Breton took her in his arms and carried her to the shore like an infant.

“I should not have said no to death—but suffering!” she murmured to Felicite, weak.

The marquise’s prostration obliged Camille to have her taken to the farmhouse from which the ladder had been borrowed. Calyste, Gasselin and Camille made a litter from spare clothes on the ladder and carried Beatrix; the farmers gave her a bed. Gasselin rode to Croisic for a doctor, telling the boatman to row to the nearest landing.

Calyste sat on a stool answering with head motions and rare monosyllables; Camille’s uneasiness at Beatrix’s condition grew with Calyste’s unnatural state. The physician bled Beatrix; she improved and consented to embark, and by five o’clock they reached the jetty at Guerande whence she was carried to Les Touches. News of the accident had already spread widely.

Calyste passed the night at Les Touches, sitting at the foot of Beatrix’s bed with Camille. The doctor had assured them that only slight stiffness would remain. Through Calyste’s despair there came a gleam of joy: he was there at her feet, could watch her sleep or wake, study her pallid face and expressions. Camille recognized in Calyste a passion one feels but once—a passion that dyes a man’s soul and faculties when nothing else distracts him. She saw he would never see the real woman in Beatrix.

How guileless Calyste was: when he saw Beatrix’s green eyes turned to him with love, confusion, and mischief, he colored and turned away.

“Did I not say truly, Calyste, that you men promise happiness and end by flinging us down a precipice?” Beatrix jested sweetly.

Calyste knelt, took her moist hand, and kissed it humbly. “You have the right to reject my love forever,” he said. “I have no right to say a word.”

“Ah!” cried Camille, seeing Beatrix’s expression and comparing it to the results of her diplomacy, “love has a wit of its own, wiser than that of all the world! Take your composing-draught, my dear friend, and go to sleep.”

That night at Les Touches left ineffaceable marks on Calyste, who had read Indiana—the work of Camille’s rival—while Mademoiselle des Touches read theological mysticism. Felicite soothed Calyste with the assurance that unless a woman were a monster she must be flattered by being the object of such a crime.

“You would never have flung me into the water,” said Camille, brushing away a tear.

Toward morning Calyste, worn out, fell asleep in his arm-chair; the marquise watched his pale, charming face and heard him murmur her name as he slept.

“He loves while sleeping,” she said to Camille.

“We must send him home,” said Felicite, waking him.

No one at the hotel du Guenic was anxious; Mademoiselle des Touches had written to the baroness about the accident.

Calyste returned to dinner at Les Touches and found Beatrix up and dressed, though pale and feeble. The harshness and coldness were gone. After an evening of music by Camille, who left Calyste free to take and press Beatrix’s hands, no storms occurred at Les Touches. Felicite completely effaced herself.

Cold, fragile, hard women like Madame de Rochefide—women whose necks have a feline turn—have pale souls; to melt them needs a thunderbolt. To Beatrix, Calyste’s fury of love and his mad action were that thunderbolt. She felt inwardly humbled; a true, pure love bathed her heart. She breathed a genial atmosphere of feelings hitherto unknown to her; she felt magnified, elevated—raised to the heaven Bretons place the Woman in. She relished the respectful adoration of the youth, whose happiness cost her little: a gesture, a look, a word satisfied him. The value Calyste placed on trifles touched her; to hold her gloved hand meant more to him than possession meant to another man. What a contrast between them!

Few women could resist such constant deification. Beatrix felt sure of being obeyed and understood; she could ask Calyste to risk his life for a caprice and he would do it without reflection. This gave her a noble and imposing air. She saw love in its grandeur and sought to remain forever the loftiest of women in his eyes.

Her coquetries grew persistent because she felt an inward weakness. She played the invalid for a week with charming hypocrisy, leaning on Calyste’s arm in languid dependence as they walked between house and garden on the velvet turf.

Before the Croisic excursion the two women had talked about love, laughing at the ways men declare it; they admitted the cleverest men, naturally the least loving, often go straight to the point. “They go to work like La Fontaine when he wanted to enter the Academy,” said Camille.

Madame de Rochefide had unbounded power to restrain Calyste by reminding him of his violence on the rocks; a look from her made his eyes fill with tears and he swallowed his prayers and arguments. By infernal coquetry she finally brought him to seek Camille’s advice.

Beatrix, armed with Calyste’s own letter, quoted his axiom that to love was the first happiness and to be loved came later; she used it to restrain his passion within respectful idolatry, which suited her. She liked the sweet hymns of praise and adoration that youth offers; she gave no definite answer. Love was not yet the question; he asked only permission to love. Calyste still clung to love’s spiritual side; that was his sublime devotion.

Nevertheless, driven to despair, Calyste complained to Camille.

“I meant to cure you by making you quickly understand her,” she replied; “but you have spoiled all. Ten days ago you were her master; to-day you are her slave. You will never have the strength to do as I advise.”

“What ought I to do?”

“Quarrel with her over her hardness. A woman is always over-excited when she discusses; let her be angry and ill-treat you, then stay away; do not return to Les Touches till she recalls you.”

In extreme illness there is a moment when the patient accepts cruel remedies. Calyste had reached that point. He followed Camille’s advice and stayed away two days; on the third he scratched at Beatrix’s door to let her know he and Camille were waiting breakfast for her.

“Another chance lost!” Camille said when she saw him reappear weakly.

During his absence Beatrix often looked through the window toward the road to Guerande. When Camille found her doing so she talked of the gorse’s golden blooms along the roadway, dazzling in the September sun.

The marquise kept them waiting long for breakfast; when she appeared, her dress showed evident intention to fascinate him and prevent another absence. After breakfast she walked with him in the garden and filled his simple heart with joy by expressing a wish to return to the rock where she had nearly perished.

“Will you go with me alone?” asked Calyste.

“If I refused, I should give you reason to think I found you dangerous. Alas! as I have told you, I belong to another, and I must be his only. I chose him knowing nothing of love. The fault was great, and bitter is my punishment.”

When she spoke thus with moist eyes, Calyste was filled with compassion that tempered his ardor; he adored her as he might a Madonna. Beatrix was undergoing an inward struggle, hesitating between herself and Calyste—between the world she hoped to re-enter and the young happiness offered to her. She began, therefore, to listen to his blind passion and allowed his soft pity to soothe her. Several times she was moved to tears as she listened and suffered him to commiserate her being bound to an evil genius like Conti. Camille had told Calyste of Beatrix’s misery in Italy and given him lectures he profited from.

“I will love you absolutely,” he said. “I have no triumphs of art to offer you; my only talent is to love you. My honor, my pride are in your perfections. No other woman can have merit in my eyes; you have no odious rival to fear. You are misconceived and wronged; for every wrong I will make you feel my comprehension day by day.”

She listened with bowed head, letting him kiss her hands and admitting silently that she was indeed an angel misunderstood.

“I am too humiliated,” she said. “My past has robbed the future of all security.”

It was a glorious day for Calyste when, arriving at Les Touches at seven, he saw Beatrix at a window watching for him, wearing the same straw hat from their memorable first excursion. For a moment he was dazzled; such small things of passion magnify the world.

They left Les Touches by the garden gate onto the dunes. Beatrix loved the sands, gathered hardy little plants with rose-colored flowers to mix with Chartreux pansies and divide with Calyste—those flowers were to be an eternal, dreadful relic.

“We’ll add a bit of box,” she said smiling.

They sat on the jetty awaiting the boat; Calyste told her of his juvenile escapade on the day of her arrival.

“I knew of your little escapade,” she said, “and it was the cause of my sternness to you that first night.”

During their walk her tone was lightly jesting with tenderness. Calyste had reason to think himself beloved. At a charming creek strewn with brilliant pebbles, when Calyste, at the summit of happiness, asked her plainly to fly with him to Ireland, she resumed her distant air, asked for his arm, and continued in silence to her Tarpeian rock.

“My friend,” she said mounting the magnificent block of granite where she made herself a pedestal, “I have not the courage to conceal what you are to me. For ten years I have had no happiness comparable to searching for shells with you and exchanging pebbles for the necklace I will treasure more than diamonds. I have been a little girl again, such as I was at fourteen or sixteen—when I was worthy of you. The love I have inspired in you has raised me in my own eyes. Understand these words.”

They reached the rock’s summit, seeing the vast ocean on one side and Brittany on the other. “But,” she continued, “I do not belong to myself; I am more bound by my own will than by law. You must be punished for my misdeed, but be satisfied to know we suffer together. Dante never saw his Beatrice again; Petrarch never had his Laura. Such disasters fall only on noble souls. But if I should be abandoned, if I fall lower into shame and ignominy and your Beatrix is cruelly misjudged, then, my adored child,” she said taking his hand, “to you she will still be first of all; you will see she rises to heaven as she leans on you; but then, if you wish to cast her down do not fail of your blow; after your love, death!”

Calyste clasped her round the waist and pressed her to his heart. As if to confirm her words, Beatrix laid a tender, timid kiss on his brow. Walking back like those who perfectly understand each other—she thinking she had gained a truce, he not doubting his happiness—both were deceived. Calyste, believing from Camille’s information that Conti might be pleased to part from Beatrix, looked to chance to arrange the future; Beatrix yielded to the vagueness of her position.

They reached Les Touches in bliss, entering by the garden gate whose key Calyste had taken. It was nearly six; the evening’s odors and sun harmonized with their feelings. Their steps were in unison, their silence full of mutual completeness. Having said all that could be said and physically tired, they walked on in silence.

Suddenly, at a turn, Beatrix was seized with a horrible trembling, the contagious horror caused by the sight of a snake, which Calyste felt before he saw the cause: on a bench beneath a weeping ash sat Conti, talking with Camille Maupin.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
The marquise’s inward, convulsive trembling showed more than she wished; a tragic drama unfolded in the souls of all present.

“You did not expect me so soon, I fancy,” said Conti, offering his arm to Beatrix.

The marquise could not avoid dropping Calyste’s arm and taking Conti’s. This ignoble transit, imperiously demanded and so dishonoring to the new love, overwhelmed Calyste, who threw himself on the bench beside Camille after exchanging the coldest salutations with his rival. Torn by conflicting emotions, he first thought to fling himself upon Conti and claim Beatrix; but the violent trembling of the woman betrayed her suffering—she had in that moment paid the penalty of her faults—and this so affected him that he was dumb, struck like her with a sense of some implacable necessity.

Madame de Rochefide and Conti passed in front of the seat where Calyste had dropped beside Camille, and as she passed the marquise gave Camille one of those terrible glances in which women say everything. She avoided Calyste’s eyes and turned to Conti, who appeared to be jesting with her.

“What will they say to each other?” Calyste asked Camille.

“Dear child, you don’t yet know the terrible rights an extinguished love still gives a man over a woman. Beatrix could not refuse to take his arm. He is surely jesting her about her new love; he must have guessed it from your attitudes and the way you approached us.”

“Jesting her!” cried the impetuous youth, starting up.

“Be calm,” Camille advised. “If he wounds her self-love, she will crush him. But Conti is too astute; he will manage her more cleverly. He will seem not to suppose that the proud Madame de Rochefide could betray him; he will represent you as an ambitious child wanting a marquise in love with him, making himself the arbiter of two women’s fates. In short, he will fire a broadside of malicious insinuations. Beatrix will be forced to parry with denials, which he will use to become once more her master.”

“Ah! he does not love her. I would leave her free. True love must be chosen anew each moment. Why did he not stay away longer? A few more days and he would not have found her. What brought him back?”

“The jest of a journalist,” replied Camille. “His opera has failed. Some journalist, probably Claude Vignon, remarked: ‘It is hard to lose fame and mistress at the same moment,’ and the speech cut him. Love based on petty sentiments is always pitiless. I have questioned him; but who can fathom so false a nature? He appeared weary of troubles and disgusted with life. He regrets his public alliance with the marquise, and spoke to me of past happiness in a melancholy poem too clever to be true. I think he hoped to worm out of me the secret of your love amid my expected flatteries.”

“What else?” said Calyste, watching Beatrix and Conti, who were coming toward them; but he no longer listened to Camille’s words.

In talking with Conti, Camille had held herself prudently on the defensive; she betrayed neither Calyste’s secret nor Beatrix’s. The great artist was capable of treachery to everyone, and Mademoiselle des Touches warned Calyste to distrust him.

“My dear friend,” she said, “this is the most critical moment for you. You need the caution and cleverness you do not possess; I fear you will be tricked by the most wily man I have ever known, and I can do nothing to help you.”

The dinner bell rang. Conti offered his arm to Camille; Calyste gave his to Beatrix. Camille stepped back to let the marquise pass, but Beatrix found a moment to look at Calyste and, by putting her finger on her lips, impressed upon him the absolute necessity of discretion.

Conti was extremely gay during dinner; perhaps this was one way of probing Madame de Rochefide, who played her part ill. If her conduct had been mere coquetry she might have deceived Conti; but her new love was real and betrayed her. The wily musician, far from increasing her embarrassment, pretended not to notice it. At dessert he steered the conversation to women and lauded the nobility of their sentiments. He said many women who might abandon a man in prosperity would sacrifice all in misfortune; women were constant, clung to their first lover as honor required, and so on. His praises, meant to wound, fell like incense on the altar where one bleeding heart lay. Camille and Beatrix alone understood the bitterness beneath the eulogy. They flushed at times, but forced themselves to control it. When dinner ended, they took each other’s arms to return to Camille’s salon, and, as if by mutual consent, turned aside into the great salon to be alone for a moment in darkness.

“It is dreadful to let Conti ride over me; yet I can’t defend myself,” said Beatrix in a low voice. “The galley-slave is always a slave to his chain-companion. I am lost; I must needs return to my galleys! And it is you, Camille, who have cast me there! Ah! you brought him back a day too soon, or a day too late. I recognize your infernal talent as author. Well, your revenge is complete, the finale perfect!”

“I may have told you I would write to Conti, but to do it was another matter,” cried Camille. “I am incapable of such baseness. But you are unhappy, and I forgive the suspicion.”

“What will become of Calyste?” asked the marquise, with naive self-conceit.

“Then Conti carries you off, does he?” Camille asked.

“Ah! you think you triumph!” cried Beatrix.

Anger distorted her handsome face as she spoke to Camille, who tried to hide satisfaction under a false expression of sympathy. The sparkle in Camille’s eyes belied the sadness of her face, and Beatrix, learned in such deceptions, saw through it. When, a few moments later, the two women sat under a strong light on that divan where the first three weeks had hosted so many comedies and where so many secret tragedies began, they examined each other for the last time and felt forever parted by undying hatred.

“Calyste remains to you,” said Beatrix, looking into Camille’s eyes; “but I am fixed in his heart, and no woman can ever drive me out of it.”

Camille replied, with an ironical tone that struck the marquise to the heart, in the famous words of Mazarin’s niece to Louis XIV: “You reign, you love, and you depart!”

Neither Camille nor Beatrix was conscious during this sharp, bitter scene of the absence of Conti and Calyste. The composer had remained at table with his rival, begging him to keep him company in finishing a bottle of champagne.

“We have something to say to each other,” added Conti, to prevent refusal.

Placed as they were, the young Breton could not refuse this challenge.

“My dear friend,” said the composer in his most caressing voice as the poor lad had drunk a couple of glasses, “we are both good fellows and can speak frankly. I have not come suspiciously. Beatrix loves me,”—with a gesture of self-conceit—“but the truth is, I have ceased to love her. I am not here to carry her off with me, but to break off our relations and leave her the honors of the rupture. You are young; you don’t yet know how useful it is to appear the victim when you are the executioner. Young men spit fire; they leave a woman with fury; they often despise her and make her hate them. But wise men do as I do; they get themselves dismissed, assuming mortification, which leaves regret in the woman’s heart and a sense of her superiority. You don’t know how hampered men often are by rash promises women accept when gallantry obliges us to make nooses for our happiness. We swear eternal faithfulness and seem to await a husband’s death impatiently; let him die, and some provincial women say: ‘Here am I, free at last.’ The spent ball comes to life again at our triumphs. I have seen that you love Beatrix. I leave her in a position where she loses nothing of her majesty; she will certainly coquet with you, if only to tease that angel Camille Maupin. Well, take her, love her—you’ll do me a great service; I want her to turn against me. I have feared her pride and virtue. Perhaps it will take time to effect this chassez-croisez. On such occasions the wisest plan is to take no step at all. I did just now, walking on the lawn, attempt to let her see I knew all and was ready to congratulate her on her new happiness. Well, she was furious! At this moment I am desperately in love with a prima donna, Mademoiselle Falcon of the Grand Opera. I think of marrying her; yes, I have got as far as that. When you come to Paris you will see I have changed a marquise for a queen.”

Calyste’s candid face revealed his satisfaction; he admitted his love for Beatrix, which was all Conti wanted to discover. No man, however blasé, will not flame up again when his love is threatened by a rival. He may wish to leave a woman, but he will never willingly let her leave him. When lovers reach this extremity, both strive for priority of action, so deep is the wound to their vanity. Questioned by the composer, Calyste related all that had happened during the last three weeks at Les Touches; Conti, concealing fury under charming good-humor, took it all in good part.

“Come, let us go upstairs,” said Conti. “Women are so distrustful; those two will wonder how we can sit here together without tearing each other’s hair out; they might even come down to listen. I’ll serve you faithfully, my dear boy. You’ll see me rough and jealous with the marquise; I shall seem to suspect her; there’s no better way to drive a woman to betray you. You will be happy, and I shall be free. Seem to pity that angel for belonging to a man without delicacy; show her a tear—for you can weep, you are young. I, alas! can weep no more; and that’s a great advantage lost.”

Calyste and Conti went up to Camille’s salon. The composer, begged by his young rival to sing, gave them the greatest of musical masterpieces viewed as execution, the famous “Pria che spunti l’aurora,” which Rubini himself never attempted without trembling, and which had often been Conti’s triumph. Never was his singing more extraordinary than on this occasion, when so many feelings contended in his breast. Calyste was in ecstasy. As Conti sang the first words of the cavatina, he looked intently at the marquise, giving the words a cruel signification fully understood. Camille, who accompanied him, guessed the order thus conveyed, which bowed the head of the luckless Beatrix. She looked at Calyste and felt sure he had fallen into some trap despite her advice. This conviction became certainty when the evidently happy Breton came up to bid Beatrix good-night, kissing her hand with a little air of happy confidence.

By the time Calyste had reached Guerande, the servants were packing Conti’s traveling carriage, and “by dawn,” as the song had said, the composer was carrying Beatrix away with Camille’s horses to the first relay. The morning twilight enabled Madame de Rochefide to see Guerande, its towers whitened by the dawn, shining out upon the still dark sky. Melancholy thoughts possessed her; she was leaving there one of the sweetest flowers of her life—a pure love, such as a young girl dreams of; the only true love she had ever known or was ever to conceive. The woman of the world obeyed the laws of the world; she sacrificed love to their demands just as many women sacrifice it to religion or duty. Sometimes mere pride can rise in acts as high as virtue. Read thus, this history is that of many women.

The next morning Calyste went to Les Touches about mid-day. From the spot where, the day before, he had seen Beatrix watching at the window, he saw Camille, who ran down to him and told the cruel truth in one word: “Gone!”

“Beatrix?” asked Calyste, thunderstruck.

“You have been duped by Conti; you told me nothing, and I could do nothing for you.”

She led him to her little salon, where he flung himself on the divan where he had so often seen the marquise, and burst into tears. Felicite smoked her hookah and said nothing, knowing well that no words could arrest the first anguish of such pain, which is always deaf and dumb. Calyste, unable even to think, much less choose a course, sat all day in complete torpidity. Just before dinner, Camille tried to speak after begging him to listen.

“Friend,” she said, “you caused me the bitterest suffering, and I had not, like you, a beautiful young life before me in which to heal myself. For me, life has no longer any spring, nor my soul a love. So, to find consolation, I have had to look above. Here, in this room, the day before Beatrix came, I drew her portrait; I did not do her injustice, or you might have thought me jealous. I wanted you to know her as she is, for that would have kept you safe. Listen now to the full truth. Madame de Rochefide is wholly unworthy of you. The scandal of her fall was unnecessary; she did it deliberately to play a part in society. She is one of those women who prefer the celebrity of scandal to tranquil happiness; they fly in the face of society to obtain the fatal alms of rebuke; they desire to be talked about at any cost. Beatrix was eaten up with vanity. Her fortune and wit had not given her the feminine royalty she craved; they had not enabled her to reign supreme over a salon. She then bethought herself of seeking the celebrity of the Duchesse de Langeais and the Vicomtesse de Beauseant. But the world gives its interest to real feelings only. Beatrix playing comedy was judged second-rate. There was no reason for her flight; the sword of Damocles was not suspended over her. She is neither sincere, loving, nor tender; if she were, would she have gone away with Conti this morning?”

Camille talked long and eloquently; but this last effort to open Calyste’s eyes was useless, and she said no more when he expressed by a gesture his absolute belief in Beatrix.

She forced him to come down into the dining-room and sit while she dined; though he could not swallow food. It is only during extreme youth that such bodily contractions occur. Later, the organs acquire fixed habits and are hardened. The reaction of the mental system upon the physical is not enough to produce mortal illness unless the physical system retains its primitive purity. A man resists violent grief that kills a youth less by the greater weakness of his affection than by the greater strength of his organs.

Mademoiselle des Touches was greatly alarmed by the calm resignation Calyste showed after his burst of tears. Before he left, he asked permission to go into Beatrix’s bedroom, where he had seen her on the night of her illness, and there he laid his head on the pillow where hers had lain.

“I am committing follies,” he said, grasping Camille’s hand, and bidding her good-night in deep dejection.

He returned home to the usual company at mouche and spent the evening sitting beside his mother. The rector, the Chevalier du Halga, and Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel all knew of Madame de Rochefide’s departure and were rejoicing. Calyste would now return to them; they watched him cautiously. No one in that old manor imagined the result of a first love in a heart so simple and true as Calyste’s.

For several days Calyste went regularly to Les Touches. He paced the lawn where he had walked with Beatrix. He often went to Croisic to stand on that fateful rock or lie for hours in the box-bush; by studying footholds he had found a means of climbing. These solitary trips, his silence, his gravity, made his mother very anxious. After about two weeks, during which this caged-animal conduct lasted, the poor lover, caged in despair, ceased to cross the bay; he had scarcely strength to drag himself along the road from Guerande to the spot where he had seen Beatrix watching. The family, delighted at the departure of “those Parisians,” saw nothing fatal or diseased about the lad. The two old maids and the rector, pursuing their scheme, kept Charlotte de Kergarouet, who nightly played coquetries on Calyste, obtaining in return nothing better than advice in playing mouche. During long evenings Calyste sat between his mother and the little Breton girl, observed by the rector and Charlotte’s aunt, who discussed his mood as they walked home. Their simple minds mistook lethargic indifference for submission to their plans. One evening when Calyste, wearied, went off to bed, the players dropped their cards and looked at each other; they had heard his steps with anxiety.

“Something is the matter with Calyste,” said the baroness, wiping her eyes.

“Nothing is the matter,” replied Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel; “but you should marry him at once.”

“Do you think marriage would divert his mind?” asked the chevalier.

Charlotte, reproving the chevalier for moral laxity, was set up to urge the match. The next day Calyste saw Charlotte, who arrived with unusual care just after the baron had given him a discourse on matrimony to which he could not answer. He now knew the ignorance of his parents and friends; he had gathered the fruit of knowledge and felt isolated. He requested a few days’ grace. The old baron rubbed his hands with joy and whispered the “good news” to the baroness.

Breakfast was gay; Charlotte, hinted to by the baron, sparkled. After the meal Calyste took Charlotte to the grotto. Their parents watched from the window with a tenderness the young man could see.

“They are watching us,” Charlotte said.

“They cannot hear us,” he replied.

“True; but they see us.”

“Let us sit down, Charlotte,” he said, taking her hand.

“Is it true your banner used to float from that twisted column?” she asked, already thinking of owning the house. “You will make changes, won’t you, Calyste?”

“I shall not have time,” he said, taking her hands and kissing them. “I love someone you have seen and who loves me, and I cannot make any other woman happy; though from childhood you and I have been destined. But I shall wait.”

“And I, too,” Charlotte said, tears filling her eyes. “You cannot long love a married woman who, they say, has gone off with a singer—”

“Marry, Charlotte,” Calyste interrupted. “With the fortune your aunt intends to give you, you could choose some better man than I. I have brought you here not to tell you what you know, but to entreat you, as a childhood friend, to take this rupture upon yourself and say you have rejected me. Say you do not wish to marry a man whose heart is not free; thus I shall be spared the sense that I have done you public wrong. You do not know how heavy life is now; I cannot bear the slightest struggle; I am like a man whose vital spark is gone. If it were not for the grief I should cause my mother, I would have flung myself into the sea. Do not speak of this to any one. Good-bye, Charlotte.”

He kissed her hair and left by the postern-gate to Les Touches, remaining near Camille till past midnight. Returning home at one, he found his mother waiting with her worsted-work. Softly he clasped her hand and asked, “Is Charlotte gone?”

“She goes tomorrow, with her aunt, in despair,” answered the baroness. “Come to Ireland with me, my Calyste.”

“Many a time I have thought of flying there—”

“Ah!” cried the baroness.

“With Beatrix,” he added.

Some days after Charlotte’s departure, Calyste joined the Chevalier du Halga for his daily promenade. They sat on a bench where his eyes could wander from Les Touches to the rocks of Croisic, against which waves dashed. Calyste was thin and pale; his strength diminished; he felt shudders at intervals denoting fever. His eyes, surrounded by dark circles, had the singular brilliancy of a fixed idea. The chevalier was the only companion with whom he could exchange a few ideas; in him Calyste divined an apostle of his own religion and recognized vestiges of an eternal love.

“Have you loved many women?” he asked.

“Only one,” the chevalier replied.

“Was she free?”

“No,” exclaimed Du Halga. “Ah! how I suffered! She was the wife of my best friend—my protector—yet we loved each other so!”

“Did she love you?” Calyste asked.

“Passionately,” the chevalier replied fervently. “Until her death; she died at forty-nine during the emigration at St. Petersburg, the climate which killed her. She must be very cold in her coffin. I have thought of fetching her to Brittany and laying her near me! But she lies in my heart.”

The chevalier brushed away his tears. Calyste took his hand and pressed it.

“I care for this little dog more than for life,” the old man said, pointing to Thisbe. “The little darling is precisely like the one she held on her knees. I never look at Thisbe but I see Madame l’Amirale’s hands.”

“Did you see Madame de Rochefide?” Calyste asked.

“No,” replied the chevalier. “It is sixty-eight years since I have looked at any woman with attention—except your mother, who has some of Madame l’Amirale’s complexion.”

Three days later the chevalier said, “My child, I have a hundred and forty louis laid by. When you know where Madame de Rochefide is, come and get them and follow her.”

Calyste thanked him, envying his existence. But day by day he grew morose; he seemed to love no one; all things hurt him; he was gentle only to his mother. The baroness watched his progress into madness with increasing anxiety; by force of prayer and entreaty she made him swallow food. Toward the end of October the lad ceased to go to the mall; the chevalier came vainly to tempt him out.

“We can talk of Madame de Rochefide,” the old man would say. “I’ll tell you my first adventure.”

“Your son is ill,” he confided to the baroness when convinced efforts were useless.

Calyste answered questions that he was well; but like all youths in melancholy, he took pleasure in the thought of death. He no longer left the house but sat in the garden on a bench, warming himself in a pale sunshine, alone with his thought and avoiding companionship.

Soon after Calyste ceased even to go to Les Touches, Felicite asked the rector to come. The assiduity with which Abbe Grimont called at Les Touches became the great topic of the town. Still, the rector never missed an evening at the hotel du Guenic, where desolation reigned. Masters and servants were afflicted by Calyste’s increasing weakness though none imagined youth might die of love. Even the chevalier had no example of such a death among his memories. They attributed Calyste’s thinness to want of food. His mother implored him to eat. Calyste tried to conquer repugnance to comfort her; nourishment taken against his will only increased the slow fever now consuming him.

During the last days of October the cherished child could no longer mount the stairs; his bed was placed in the lower hall, surrounded by family. They finally sent for the Guerande physician, who broke the fever with quinine and reduced it in a few days, ordering Calyste to take exercise and find some amusement. The baron, roused from apathy, recovered some strength; with Calyste, Gasselin, and two dogs he started for the forest, and for days they hunted. Calyste obeyed, visiting neighbors’ chateaus. But he had no spirit or gaiety; nothing brought a smile. His livid, contracted features betrayed utter passivity. The baron, worn by exertion, returned home exhausted with Calyste almost as ill. For several days both were dangerously ill; the family sent, at the Guerande physician’s request, for two of the best doctors in Nantes.

The baron had received a fatal shock on realizing the change in Calyste. With the lucidity of the dying he trembled that his race was about to perish. He prayed, and his face turned constantly toward his son’s bed. At the least motion Calyste made, a commotion stirred within him. The baroness no longer left the room where Zephirine sat knitting in horrible uneasiness. Demands were made for wood and provisions; Zephirine, not nimble enough, gave her keys to Mariotte. But she insisted on knowing everything; she questioned Mariotte and her sister-in-law. One night, when father and son dozed, Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel told Zephirine she must resign herself to the baron’s death. The old woman dropped her knitting, groped, and drew from her pocket an old black chaplet, on which she prayed with a fervor that gave her withered face a new splendor; the other old woman imitated her, and all present, on a sign from the rector, joined in the spiritual uplift.

“Alas! I prayed to God,” said the baroness, remembering her prayer after reading Calyste’s fatal letter, “and he did not hear me.”

“Perhaps it would be well,” said the rector, “to beg Mademoiselle des Touches to come see Calyste.”

“She!” cried Zephirine, “the author of all our misery! She turned him from his family, taught him impious books, an heretical language! Let her be accursed! She has destroyed the du Guenics!”

“She may perhaps restore them,” the rector said gently. “Mademoiselle des Touches is a saintly woman; I am her surety. She has only good intentions toward Calyste.”

“Let me know the day she sets foot in this house, that I may get out of it,” cried the old woman passionately. “She has killed both father and son. Do you not hear death in Calyste’s voice? He is so feeble he barely whispers.”

At that moment the three doctors arrived. They plied Calyste with questions; their examination of the baron was short; they were surprised he still lived. The Guerande doctor calmly told the baroness that for Calyste it would probably be best to take him to Paris to consult the most experienced physicians; it would cost over a hundred louis.

“People die of something, but not of love,” said Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel.

“Alas! whatever be the cause, Calyste is dying,” said the baroness. “I see all the symptoms of consumption, that most horrible disease of my country, about him.”

“Calyste dying!” said the baron, opening his eyes; two large tears rolled slowly down his wrinkled cheeks—the only tears he had probably ever shed. Suddenly he rose, walked to his son’s bedside, took his hand, and looked earnestly at him.

“What is it you want, father?” said Calyste.

“That you should live!” cried the baron.

“I cannot live without Beatrix,” replied Calyste.

The old man dropped into a chair.

“Oh! where could we get a hundred louis to bring doctors from Paris? There is still time,” cried the baroness.

“A hundred louis!” cried Zephirine; “will that save him?”

Without waiting, the old maid ran her hands into her placket-holes, unfastened her petticoat, and let it fall, producing a heavy sound as coins tumbled. She knew where she had sewn her savings and ripped them out with the rapidity of magic. The gold pieces rang as they fell, one by one, into her lap. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel gazed in amazement.

“But they’ll see you!” she whispered.

“Thirty-seven,” answered Zephirine, counting.

“Every one will know how much you have.”

“Forty-two.”

“Double louis! all new! How did you get them, you who can’t see clearly?”

“I felt them. Here’s one hundred and four louis,” cried Zephirine. “Is that enough?”

“What is all this?” asked the Chevalier du Halga, coming in, puzzled by the old blind friend holding a petticoat full of gold.

Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoel explained.

“I knew it,” said the chevalier. “And I have come to bring a hundred and forty louis I have been holding at Calyste’s disposal, as he knows.”

The chevalier produced rouleaux from his pocket. Mariotte, seeing such wealth, sent Gasselin to lock the doors.

“Gold will not give him health,” said the baroness, weeping.

“But it can take him to Paris, where he can find her. Come, Calyste.”

“Yes,” cried Calyste, springing up, “I will go.”

“He will live,” said the baron, in a shaking voice; “and I can die—send for the rector!”

The words cast terror on all present. Calyste, seeing the mortal paleness on his father’s face, came to his side. The rector, after hearing the doctors, had gone to Mademoiselle des Touches intending to bring her back; in proportion as he had formerly detested her, he now admired and protected her as a shepherd protects the most precious of his flock.

When news of the baron’s approaching end spread in Guerande, a crowd gathered; peasants, paludiers, and servants knelt in the courtyard while the rector administered last sacraments to the old Breton warrior. The whole town was agitated by the probable extinction of this old Breton race; it was felt as a public calamity.

The solemn ceremony affected Calyste deeply. His filial sorrow silenced the anguish of his love for a moment. During the baron’s last hour he knelt beside him watching death come. The old man died in his chair in presence of the assembled family.

“I die faithful to God and his religion,” he said. “My God! as the reward of my efforts grant that Calyste may live!”

“I shall live, father, and I will obey you,” said the young man.

“If you wish to make my death as happy as Fanny has made my life, swear to me to marry.”

“I promise it, father.”

It was touching to see Calyste, or rather his shadow, leaning on the chevalier’s arm—a spectre leading a shade—following the baron’s coffin as chief mourner. The church and little square were crowded with country people from thirty miles round.

But the baroness and Zephirine soon saw that despite his promise Calyste fell back into fatal stupor. On the day the family donned mourning the baroness took her son to a garden bench and questioned him closely. Calyste answered gently and submissively, but his replies proved only the despair of his soul.

“Mother,” he said, “there is no life in me. What I eat does not feed me; the air does not refresh me; the sun feels cold; it seems to you to light that front of the house, but to me it is a blur. If Beatrix were here it would be dazzling. There is but one thing left that keeps its shape to my eyes—this flower,” adding he drew from his breast the withered bunch the marquise had given him at Croisic.

The baroness dared not say more. His answer seemed more indicative of madness than his silence. She saw no hope or light in the darkness.

The baron’s last hours had prevented the rector from bringing Mademoiselle des Touches to Calyste. But that day, while mother and son sat on the bench, Calyste quivered at seeing Felicite through the opposite windows. She reminded him of Beatrix, and his life revived. It was to Camille the poor mother owed the first motion of joy that lightened her mourning.

“Well, Calyste,” said Mademoiselle des Touches, “I want you to go to Paris with me. We will find Beatrix,” she added in a low voice.

The pale, thin face flushed red and a smile brightened him.

“Let us go,” he said.

“We shall save him,” said Mademoiselle des Touches to the mother, who pressed her hands and wept for joy.

A week after the baron’s funeral, Mademoiselle des Touches, the Baronne du Guenic, and Calyste started for Paris, leaving the household in charge of old Zephirine.
################################################
############# MIDDLE OF THE STORY. #############
################################################
Felicite’s tender love was preparing for Calyste a prosperous future. Being allied to the Grandlieu family—the ducal line would end in five daughters for lack of a male heir—she had written to the Duchesse de Grandlieu describing Calyste, his history, and her intentions. She had lately sold her house in the rue du Mont-Blanc for two million five hundred thousand francs. Her agent bought for her a charming new house in the rue de Bourbon for seven hundred thousand francs; one million she intended to devote to recovering the du Guenic estates, and the rest she desired to settle upon Sabine de Grandlieu. Felicite knew the Grandlieus’ plans: the youngest was to marry the Vicomte de Grandlieu (the heir); Clotilde-Frédérique, the second daughter, wished to remain unmarried in memory of Lucien de Rubempré, while not becoming a nun like her eldest sister; two sisters were already married; and Sabine, nearly twenty and the second youngest, was the only available daughter. Felicite resolved that Sabine should cure Calyste of his passion for Béatrix.

During the journey to Paris Mademoiselle des Touches revealed these arrangements to the baroness. The new rue de Bourbon house was being decorated and intended as Sabine and Calyste’s home if the plan succeeded.

The party stayed at the hôtel de Grandlieu, where the baroness was received with the distinction due to her rank as a du Guenic and the daughter of a British peer. Mademoiselle des Touches urged Calyste to see Paris, investigated Béatrix (who had disappeared abroad), and threw him into diversions and amusements. The season for balls was beginning, and the duchess and her daughters did the honors. Calyste found some resemblance between Madame de Rochefide and Sabine, one of the handsomest and most charming girls in Parisian society; this fancied likeness made him attend willingly to Sabine’s coquetries. Sabine liked Calyste, and the winter of 1837 passed so that the young baron—his youth and health restored—listened without repugnance to his mother when she reminded him of the promise to his dying father and proposed marriage with Sabine. He agreed outwardly but kept within himself an indifference known only to the baroness, who trusted it would be conquered by a happy home.

On the day the families gathered in the Grandlieu salon to sign the marriage contract, when the notary Leopold Hannequin began preliminaries, Calyste—clouded—suddenly and curtly refused to accept the benefactions offered by Mademoiselle des Touches. Did he still rely on Felicite’s devotion to recover Béatrix? In the embarrassment of the assembled families, Sabine entered and handed him a letter from Mademoiselle des Touches, to be given on that occasion.

Calyste read:

Camille Maupin to Calyste.

Calyste—before I enter my convent cell I am permitted to cast a last look upon the world I leave for a life of prayer and solitude. That look is for you, who have been my whole world these last months. My voice will reach you at the moment of a ceremony in which I cannot share.

On the day you stand before the altar giving your hand to a young and charming girl who can love you openly, I shall be at another altar in a convent at Nantes betrothed forever to Him who neither fails nor betrays. I do not write to sadden you, but to entreat you not to deny the service I wish to do you. Do not contest my rights so dearly bought.

If love is suffering, I have loved you indeed, my Calyste. But have no remorse; the only happiness I have known I owe to you; my pangs sprang from myself. Make me recompense by causing me an everlasting joy. Let poor Camille, who is no longer what she was, still be something in the material comfort you enjoy. Let me be like a fragrance in your life, unseen and not importunate.

To you, Calyste, I shall owe my eternal happiness; will you not accept a few paltry and fleeting benefits from me? Do not be wanting in generosity. Do you not see this as the last message of a renounced love? The world without you held nothing more for me; you made it the most awful of solitudes; you have brought Camille Maupin, the unbeliever and writer, bound before God.

I am now what I might have been—innocent, a child. I have washed my robes in tears of repentance; I can come before the altar to which my guardian angel, my beloved Calyste, has led me. I love you without self-seeking, as a mother loves her son, as the Church loves her children. I can pray for you and yours without wish except your happiness. If you knew the sublime tranquillity in which I live, now risen above petty interests and doing our duty, you would enter your new life with unfaltering steps and never a look behind. Society cannot exist without the religion of duty; sacrifice your fancy to your duties as head of your race, husband, father. Raise the fallen standard of the old du Guenics; show what a gentleman is in honor and grandeur. Let me play a mother’s part to you; your own mother will not be jealous of this voice from a tomb, these hands uplifted to heaven imploring blessings. Today, more than ever, rank and nobility need fortune. Calyste, accept a part of mine, and make a worthy use of it. It is not a gift but a trust I place in you. I thought more of your children and the old Breton house than of you in offering you my Parisian profits.

“Let us now sign the contract,” said the young baron, returning to the company.

The Abbé Grimont, to whom Camille’s conversion was attributed, soon became vicar-general of the diocese.

The following week, after a ceremony at seven in the morning at Saint-Thomas d’Aquin, Calyste and Sabine left in their pretty travelling-carriage amid tears, embraces, and congratulations. Congratulations came from the witnesses and men; tears from the Duchesse de Grandlieu and Clotilde, who trembled thinking, “She is launched upon the sea of life! Poor Sabine! at the mercy of a man who does not marry entirely of his own free will.”

Marriage is not wholly pleasures; it involves temperamental compatibility, physical sympathies, and character harmonies. Mothers and marriageable daughters know the lottery’s terms and dangers—that is why women weep at a wedding while men smile; men think they risk nothing, women know, or nearly know, what they risk.

In a preceding carriage rode the Baronne du Guenic, to whom the duchess had said, “You are a mother, though you have only had one son; try to take my place to my dear Sabine.” On the bridal carriage sat a chasseur as courier; two waiting-maids and four postilions, decorated with ribbons and bouquets (which the duke bribed them to relinquish) completed the convoy. The duchess bade them farewell, hinting to Calyste that she understood him. Clotilde watched them depart, tearful, as the convoy clattered off toward Brittany.

It is singular that both artisans and great families set out publicly after marriage: the great hide themselves in carriages; the little people parade roads and woods. A moralist might debate which modesty is finer—the private inauguration of the domestic hearth or the public display. The love which begins a marriage is a jewel to be buried in the depths of the soul.

Who can relate a honeymoon? Much of it belongs to the bride. Many women will recognize that this uncertain period forecasts conjugal life. Young women of the faubourg Saint-Germain, like Sabine, are, if intelligent, well trained in manners and bearing; such upbringing, combined with virgin sincerity, made Sabine as interesting as Mademoiselle de Chaulieu. Her letters to her mother during the honeymoon show her mind and temperament.

Guerande, April, 1838.

To Madame la Duchesse de Grandlieu:

Dear Mamma,—You will understand why I did not write during the journey—our wits are like wheels. Here I am in the depths of Brittany at the hôtel du Guenic—a house carved like a sandal-wood box. Despite the family’s affection, I feel a desire to fly to you and confess things only a mother should know.

Calyste married with great sorrow. We all knew that, and you warned me of difficulties; but they are greater than you thought. Ah, my dear mother, what experience we gain in hours! All your counsels seemed fruitless; I love Calyste as if he were not my husband—that is, if I were married to another and traveling with Calyste, I should love Calyste and hate my husband.

Now imagine a man loved so completely, involuntarily, absolutely; my servitude is established despite your good advice. You told me to be grand, dignified, and self-respecting to win esteem and consideration that sanctify a woman. I remember your warnings about the young women who begin marriage with compliance and familiarity that lead to indifference and contempt. “Remember that you are a Grandlieu!” Yes, I recall all that.

But could you have supposed I should begin with the catastrophe you feared? When Calyste and I were alone in the carriage we felt foolish, both bewildered by the first words and looks; at the barrier he began the set speech one makes—“My dear Sabine, I want you happy in your own way; let us be at once what we shall be years hence; think that you have a friend and brother in me, as I shall have a sister and friend in you.” It was said with delicacy, but it did not answer my feelings; I replied that I felt the same sentiments, and we talked of weather and scenery.

At Versailles I asked him to tell me the events that led him to death and to which I owed my marriage. My request caused an argument through three relays—me obstinate, him debating like a king asked to yield. At last, after Verneuil and vows, he related his love for Madame de Rochefide.

“I do not wish to have any secrets between us,” he said.

Poor Calyste—he did not know you and Mademoiselle des Touches had told me the truth. You will not blame me, mother, for reconnoitring the wound you warned me of. Eight hours after Saint-Thomas d’Aquin I found myself in the false position of a young wife hearing from her husband the story of his misplaced love for another. Still, I gained what I sought. He ended his tale with warm protestations of absolute forgetting. He took my hand, kissed it, and kept it long clasped. A declaration followed—not in words but by gestures. Perhaps I owed it to the indignation I felt at the bad taste of a woman foolish enough not to love my glorious Calyste.

They are calling me to play cards—I will finish tomorrow. Adieu.

Your Sabine.

Guerande, May, 1838.

I take up my Odyssey. On the third day we dropped ceremonious formality and thee’d and thou’d like lovers. My mother-in-law, trying to take your place, has been so charming she is almost like you. I think she guessed my heroism and hid her anxiety in visible precaution.

When I saw the towers of Guerande I whispered to your son-in-law, “Have you really forgotten her?” Calyste, now my angel, grew wild with happiness at my words. The desire to put Madame de Rochefide forever from his mind led me perhaps too far. But how could I help it? I love, and I am somewhat Portuguese—I am more like you than my father.

Calyste accepts all from me as an only child thinks everything his right. Between ourselves, I will not give my daughter to an only son; I see tyrants in an only son. We have inverted our parts: I am the devoted half. Devotion has dangers—loss of dignity; but what can I do? You were not here, and I saw a gulf. Had I kept my dignity I might have won only the cold joys of a brotherhood that would drift into indifference. My devotion has made me Calyste’s slave; shall I regret it? We shall see.

For now I love him absolutely with a mother’s folly.

Guerande, May 15th.

To the present, dear mamma, marriage is delightful. I spend all my tenderness on the noblest of men whom a foolish woman disdained for a fiddler—she was a fool, a cold fool. I, in my legitimate love, am charitable; I cure his wounds and lay my heart open to incurable ones. The more I love Calyste the more I fear losing this present happiness.

The whole family and circle at the hôtel de Guenic adore me. They are personages born under high tapestries—antiquated and noble. When alone I will describe Aunt Zéphirine, Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël, the Chevalier du Halga, and others. Even the two servants, Gasselin and Mariotte (whom I wish to take to Paris), regard me as an angel.

My mother-in-law has installed us in the apartments she and her late husband occupied. She gave us her rooms with touching words: “I spent my married life, a happy woman, in these rooms; may the omen be happy for you.” She has taken Calyste’s room. Saintly soul! She seems intent on investing us with her memories. Brittany, its customs, and its feudal faith have something sacred even in trifles.

All the tenants of the du Guenic estates—bought back by Mademoiselle des Touches—came in holiday dress to pay respects. They rejoiced that Calyste was now really their master; they understand Brittany, the feudal system, and old France. The leases have been proposed by the “gars” themselves; we shall sign them after inspecting estates mortgaged for a century and a half. Mademoiselle de Pen-Hoël told me the “gars” have reckoned rentals with a justice Parisians would hardly believe. We start in three days on horseback. For now my happiness is at its height.

Nantes, June, 1838.

After playing chatelaine—riding through forests, halting at farmhouses, dining on oaken tables with centenary linen, drinking wines to volleys of musketry, attending bagpipe balls, receiving bouquets and acclamations, and sleeping deeply with love’s delightful awakenings—we are at Nantes.

What a day at the old castle! The rector and clergy received us crowned with flowers and blessed us; I wept at the joy. Calyste played his seigneur like a Walter Scott figure; he received tenants’ homage as if in the thirteenth century. The peasants called him “a beautiful seigneur,” and old men likened him to former Guenics. Noble, sublime Brittany! Progress will bring bridges and roads; perhaps the peasants will cease to be as proud as I saw them now.

After signing contracts and leases we came here to kneel with our happiness at the feet of her who gave it to us.

In memory of her, Calyste has quartered his arms with those of Des Touches; he means to take an eagle argent supporter and put the motto “Souviegne-vous” in its beak.

Yesterday we visited the convent of the Visitation ladies with the Abbé Grimont, who told us Felicite was indeed a saint. Mademoiselle des Touches declined to receive Calyste and would only see me. She looked thinner and paler but pleased at our visit.

“Tell Calyste,” she said low, “it is a matter of conscience with me not to see him. I prefer not to buy happiness by months of suffering. The mother of the novices cannot conceive the number and extent of ideas that rush through my mind when she asks, ‘Of what are you thinking?’ Sometimes I see Italy or Paris, but always Calyste is the sun of memory to me.” She explained she had entered the order of Saint-François de Sales because its rule disciplined the intellect rather than mortified the body—she sought austerity toward the mind. “My head was the guilty part,” she said, “it deceived me as to my heart until the fatal age of forty, when for brief moments we are forty times happier than young women and soon fifty times more unhappy. But tell me—are you happy?”

“You see me under all the enchantments of love,” I answered.

“Calyste is as good and simple as he is noble and beautiful,” she said gravely. “I have made you my heiress in more than property; you possess the double ideal I dreamed. I rejoice in what I have done. But make no mistake; do yourself no wrong. You have easily won happiness; you have only to stretch out your hand to take it; but be careful to preserve it. If you had come merely to carry away the counsels my knowledge of your husband gives, the journey would be well repaid. Calyste is moved now by a communicated passion, but you have not inspired it. To make your happiness lasting, try to give him something of his former emotions. In the interests of both, be capricious, be coquettish; you must be. I am not advising odious scheming, but the science of a woman’s life. Between usury and prodigality, child, is economy. Acquire honorably an empire over Calyste. These are the last words on earthly interests I shall ever utter; I have kept them to say as we part. Bind him to you, give him children, let him respect their mother in you—and,” she added in a low trembling voice, “if you can, see that he never again meets Béatrix.”

That name plunged us both into stupor. We exchanged uneasy looks.

“Do you return to Guerande?” she asked.

“Yes,” I said.

“Never go to Les Touches. I did wrong to give him that property.”

“Why?” I asked.

“Child! Les Touches is Bluebeard’s chamber for you. Nothing is so dangerous as to wake a sleeping passion.”
